Edward Blum is a conservative legal strategist and the founder/president of Students for Fair Admissions (SFFA).

  • Heâ€™s not a lawyer but has engineered several landmark Supreme Court cases (e.g., Shelby County v. Holder on the Voting Rights Act).
  • His mission is to eliminate racial classifications in education, voting, and employment.
  • His critics argue heâ€™s promoting a colorblind legal ideology that ignores structural inequity; his supporters view him as defending individual merit and legal neutrality.

What the fuck is he thinking?
Blum believes the Constitution requires a race-neutral society, where race should not be used by public institutions at all, even to remedy past or current discrimination. He sees affirmative action as a form of reverse discrimination.

âœ³ï¸ Reaffirmed 4 Key Limits on Race-Conscious Programs:

  1. No use of race as a negative: A program cannot disadvantage individuals based on their race (e.g., Asian Americans).
  2. No reinforcement of stereotypes: Race must not be used in a way that assumes anything about a personâ€™s traits or abilities.
  3. Must be measurably connected to goals: Abstract invocations of â€œdiversityâ€ are not enoughâ€”there must be clear metrics and endpoints.
  4. Race-conscious programs must have a logical endpoint: Permanent race-based policies are unconstitutional. Diversity cannot be so vague that schools never know when theyâ€™ve achieved it.

Despite public confusion, the ruling does not ban all attempts to increase diversity:

Permissible Strategies Examples
Race-neutral programs % plans (e.g., top 10% of each high school), SES-based admissions, adversity scoring, first-gen preference
Holistic review of racialized experiences Applicants may still write about how race shaped their identity, struggle, or valuesâ€”but race itself cannot be the determining factor
Increased need-based aid More financial aid for low-income applicants
Redefining â€œmeritâ€ Moving beyond test scores to include community service, leadership, overcoming hardship
Removing structural barriers Dropping standardized tests, legacy preferences, app fees
Campus DEIA programs Support for affinity groups, anti-discrimination systems, diverse faculty hiring

âŒ Is this truly about merit?

Many critics argue the decision undermines holistic admissions by ignoring how race intersects with:

  • Wealth & poverty
  • Generational disadvantage
  • Neighborhood & school segregation
  • Trauma and resilience

Some scholars argue this returns admissions to a false â€œneutralâ€ standardâ€”one that historically benefited wealthy, white, and legacy students.

Is â€œcolorblindnessâ€ just?
Is it justice as formal equality (treating everyone the same), or justice as substantive equality (giving people what they need to thrive)?

...to the point where we see that race is a fact, but not the defining fact and not the fact that tells us what is important, but we are not there yet. Until we are, race-conscious admissions programs that survive strict scrutiny will have an important place in society and help ensure that colleges and universities can offer a diverse atmosphere that fosters learning, improves scholarship, and encourages mutual respect and understanding. -- District Court Judge Allison Burrough

Additional Context

ğŸ”¹ First Amendment Doctrinal Categories â€“ Exceptions to Protection

Category Standard / Case When Speech Is Unprotected Typical Application Contexts
Fighting Words Chaplinsky v. N.H. (1942) Face-to-face, personal insults likely to provoke immediate retaliatory violence Street confrontations, protests, bar fights (rarely upheld today)
True Threats Virginia v. Black (2003), Counterman (2023) A serious expression of intent to commit violence, made with knowledge or recklessness Threats to President, stalking, online threats, violent domestic abuse
Incitement to Lawless Action Brandenburg v. Ohio (1969) Speech directed to inciting imminent lawless conduct and likely to produce it Extremist rallies, mob agitation, terror propaganda
Harassment (Work/School) Meritor Savings v. Vinson (1986), Title IX Severe, pervasive, and objectively offensive conduct interfering with education/employment Sexual, racial, gender-based verbal conduct in schools or workplaces
Defamation (Libel/Slander) New York Times v. Sullivan (1964) False factual assertions causing reputational harm; public figures must show actual malice Press, social media, political campaigns, personal attacks
Obscenity Miller v. California (1973) Appeals to prurient interest, depicts sexual conduct patently offensive, lacks value Pornography prosecutions (mostly obsolete with Internet age)
Child Pornography New York v. Ferber (1982) Any depiction of minors in sexual conduct; no value test needed Possession, production, or distribution involving real children
Commercial Speech (Limited) Central Hudson (1980) May be regulated if misleading, unlawful, or the regulation meets intermediate scrutiny Ads for illegal goods, misleading health claims

ğŸ”¹ Key Distinctions Between Categories

Doctrine Whatâ€™s Being Provoked Core Test Protected Ifâ€¦
Fighting Words Violence from the listener toward speaker Face-to-face insult likely to provoke retaliation General vulgarity not directed at a person
Incitement (Provoke) Unlawful action by the listener -- provoking violence against the speaker Intent + imminence + likelihood (Brandenburg) No immediate danger, abstract ideology
True Threat Fear or terror in the target Serious intent to harm + subjective awareness (Black, Counterman) Conditional or hyperbolic political speech
Harassment Systematic abuse within institutions Severe, pervasive, objectively offensive Single or offhand comment in public
Defamation **Reputational injury through false speech False statement of fact + damage + culpability (Sullivan) Opinion, parody, hyperbole are protected

ğŸŸ¢ Application Framework â€“ Watts v. U.S. (1969) Factors for â€œTrue Threatsâ€

Watts Factors Explanation
Context of Speech Political rally vs. private threat vs. online comment
Audience Reaction Was it laughed off, ignored, or taken seriously?
Conditional or Not â€œIf they draft me, thenâ€¦â€ â†’ suggests lack of immediacy or seriousness

âœ… Watts: â€œIf they ever make me carry a rifle, the first man I want to get in my sights is LBJ.â€
ğŸ” Context = political protest; Audience = laughed; Threat = conditional â†’ Protected hyperbole.

ğŸŸ¢ Key Case: R.A.V. v. St. Paul (1992) â€” Viewpoint Discrimination within Unprotected Categories

  • Held: Even within an unprotected category (e.g., fighting words), the government cannot engage in content- or viewpoint-based discrimination.
R.A.V. Principle
A regulation cannot criminalize only certain views within fighting words or threats (e.g., only racist epithets) unless it targets a neutral, proscribable basis (e.g., threats to safety).

ğŸ” E.g., You can't criminalize cross-burning with racist motivation but allow it for other motives, unless you're regulating true threats (Virginia v. Black carved out this nuance).

ğŸŸ¢ Key Case:â€¯Virginiaâ€¯v.â€¯Blackâ€¯(2003)â€¯â€” â€œTrueâ€‘Threatâ€ Definition and Permissible Regulation

Virginia v.â€¯Black Principle
The State may punish speech as a true threat only when the speaker
(1) directs the statement toward a specific individual or clearly identifiable group and
(2) conveys a serious expression of intent to commit unlawful violence and
(3) acts with the purpose (or conscious awareness) of placing the target in fear of bodily harm or death. The speaker need not actually intend to follow through; the constitutional interest is in shielding the target from fear, the disruption that fear causes, and the possibility that the threatened violence might occur.

1. Millerâ€¯v.â€¯California,â€¯413â€¯U.S.â€¯15â€¯(1973)

Senate gallery during session Limited public forum; decorum rules long-recognized. You can be expelled for violating the gallery dress code; but a blanket ban on criticism of senators while allowing â€œPro-Senatorâ€ garb would be invalid viewpoint discrimination.
Inside a courtroom during proceedings Non-public forum; judge holds inherent contempt power. Court may bar disruptive or disrespectful attire to preserve neutrality and decorum. Nebulous â€œoffensivenessâ€ is enough here. Likely permissible to eject or sanction; Cohen yields to the judiciaryâ€™s stronger order-maintenance interest.

Key doctrinal hooks: Cohen; Perry Educ. Assâ€™n; Madsen (court orders); courtroom contempt precedents. No obscenity (fails Miller prongs), no fighting words (not a direct, face-to-face insult).

2. â€œF* Trump/President!â€ billboard on a pickup truck

  • Forum: public streets â†’ traditional public forum for speech attached to private property.
  • Category: core political viewpoint; not obscene, not fighting words (no personal addressee).
  • Rule: Any enforcement (ticket for â€œobscenity,â€ zoningâ€sign ordinance, or disorderly-conduct statute) must be content-neutral. Cases challenging â€œF*** Bidenâ€ decals have uniformly favored the speaker because Miller obscenity fails at the first prong (no appeal to prurient sexual interest).
  • Caveat: A genuinely neutral size-or-traffic-safety sign ordinance (Reed v. Town of Gilbert) can require smaller lettering, but it cannot single out profanity or a viewpoint.

3. â€œDisorderly-conductâ€ catch-alls for loitering, drunkenness, public urination

Conduct Speech? 1A Shield? Bottom Line
Loitering / public intoxication Typically non-expressive conduct; Oâ€™Brien symbolic-speech test rarely satisfied. Minimal First-Amendment coverage. Statutes OK if they have a clear mens rea and do not target a viewpoint (e.g., City of Chicago v. Morales struck down for vagueness, not for speech).
Public urination / lewd exposure Pure conduct, or at most low-value indecent speech. Even if construed as expression, it would likely fit the secondary effects / public-health rationaleâ€”highly regulable. State can criminalize without implicating Cohenâ€“Miller concerns.
â€œDisorderly conductâ€ applied to chanting, picketing, swearing Speech; triggers scrutiny. Must satisfy Brandenburg (incitement) or Chaplinsky (fighting words) or be a valid time-place-manner rule. Mere profanity in public (without imminent violence) is protected; vague â€œoffensivenessâ€ provisions are void (Coates).

4. Library book curation and removal

  • Public libraries are viewed as limited public fora devoted to the communication of speech. Under Board of Educ. v. Pico and circuit cases:
    • Content-based but viewpoint-neutral weeding (outdated science texts, duplicate copies) is permissible.
    • Viewpoint discrimination (removing all pro-LGBTQ titles, or exclusively â€œanti-colonialâ€ works) is presumptively unconstitutional.
    • Schools get slightly more leeway than public libraries, but even so, political viewpoints may not be excised solely for being â€œinappropriate.â€

5. Rainbow flags; â€œAllahu Akbar!â€; â€œFrom the river to the seaâ€; â€œImpeach him,â€ etc.

5.5 Auto-Felon Quartet:

â€œF* these politicians/activists! Someone really ought to blow up the office building / HBCU / Black church!â€ Advocacy of violence â†’ Brandenburg incitement test. Speech is protected unless (i) intended to spur imminent lawless action and (ii) objectively likely to do so. Vague â€œsomeone shouldâ€ + no timetable = usually not imminent. Brandenburg v. Ohio; Hess v. Indiana (1973) (future-tense advocacy protected). Possible federal scrutiny under 18 U.S.C. Â§ 875(c) (interstate threat) or hate-crime investigations. Probably protected, but expect FBI knock & grand-jury subpoena if posted online; prosecution only if facts show concrete, imminent plot.
â€œI will bomb the White House / plane / school / mall / office building!â€ True threatâ€”speaker expresses a serious intent to commit violence. Not protected. Virginia v. Black (2003); Watts v. U.S. (1969) (political hyperbole line). 18 U.S.C. Â§ 871 (Pres./VP threats), Â§ 844(e) (bomb threats by any medium), Â§ 875(c) (interstate threats), Â§ 115 (threat against U.S. officials). Felony indictment almost automatic; no need to prove ability to carry it out. Secret Service or ATF leads the arrest.
â€œI will bomb ___ on XX date, XX time.â€ True threat + hoax bomb threat. Specific time makes it credible â†’ triggers evacuations. Same as above plus 18 U.S.C. Â§ 1038(a) (terroristic-hoax statute) and state â€œfalse reportâ€ laws. Felony; sentencing higher because emergency services are mobilized; restitution for evacuation costs common.
â€œThanks to X, Iâ€™ve built a [named] bomb and will deploy it at ___ on XX date, XX time.â€ True threat + confession of terrorist conspiracy / material support. No First-Amendment shelter. 18 U.S.C. Â§ 2332a (use of WMD), Â§ 2332b (terrorism transcending national boundaries), Â§ 371 (conspiracy), Â§ 842(p) (instruction on explosives), Â§ 2339A/B (material support). Immediate arrest on multiple federal counts; potential life sentence. Speech itself is the overt act proving conspiracy.

Special federal protection for high officials

  • 18 U.S.C. Â§ 871 â€“ threats, knowingly and willfully â€œto kill, kidnap, or inflict bodily harmâ€ upon the President, Vice President, or those in the line of succession.
  • 18 U.S.C. Â§ 879 â€“ extends similar protection to former Presidents, major candidates, and their families.
  • 18 U.S.C. Â§ 1751 â€“ makes actual assaults on the President/VP a separate federal offense.

When you say â€œI will bomb the White House:â€

  • You're arrested under 18 U.S.C. Â§ 871 before lunch.
  • Your computer and phone are seized by federal agents.
  • A prosecutor gets an indictment without ever applying Brandenburg.
  • You sit in a cell unless you can afford bail, which is often denied in threat cases.
  • If you fight it, you're spending $100,000+ and 2+ years hoping a judge finds it wasn't a â€œtrue threat.â€

==Clearly, while one may freely engage in discussions about the application of these constitutional tests and principles in the classroom setting, in reality, ANY relevant speech can result in immediate felony charges.==

6. Broadcast-specific indecency regulation

  • FCC v. Pacifica (1978) recognized a narrow, medium-specific carve-out for â€œindecentâ€ (not obscene) speech on broadcast radio/television during hours when children are likely to be listening (the â€œsafe-harborâ€ rule).
  • Rationale: pervasiveness + easy accessibility to children.
  • Cable, the Internet, streaming â†’ protected platforms (Reno v. ACLU, U.S. v. Playboy)â€”Pacifica does not extend automatically.

7. K-12 schools v. colleges

Case Rule Applies Where Standard
Tinker (1969) Student political speech that is neither vulgar nor school-sponsored School must show â€œmaterial and substantial disruption.â€
Fraser (1986) Lewd, sexually explicit student speech on campus Per se punishable; no Tinker disruption showing required.
Hazelwood (1988) School-sponsored curricular speech (newspaper, play) Educators may control so long as decision is reasonably related to legitimate pedagogical concerns.

Universities Adults; Papish (1973); Rosenberger School may not employ Fraser-style lewdness rules; full public-forum analysis.

8. Government-employee & teacher speech

  1. Pickering v. Board of Education (1968)
    Outside-duty, public-concern speech â†’ apply Pickering balancing (employee interest vs. workplace efficiency).
  2. Connick v. Myers (1983)
    If the speech is purely private grievance, employer may discipline without First-Amendment scrutiny.
  3. Garcetti v. Ceballos (2006)
    Speech made pursuant to official job duties is not protected at all.
    Professorial academic freedom caveat left undecided; lower courts split.

Teachers disciplining students may invoke Fraser/Hazelwood controls, but their own speech outside class about politics is largely Pickering-protected.

9. Putting it all together â€“ the decision tree

  1. Is it speech or conduct?
    â€“ Pure conduct (urination, intoxication) â†’ ordinary police power.
    â€“ Expressive conduct â†’ apply Oâ€™Brien or Johnson.
  2. Does it fit a categorical exclusion?
    â€“ Miller obscenity, Ferber child porn, Brandenburg incitement, Chaplinsky fighting words, true threat (Virginia v. Black), defamation, fraud.
  3. Is there a context-specific carve-out?
    â€“ Schools (Tinker/Fraser/Hazelwood/Morse/Mahanoy)
    â€“ Broadcast (Pacifica)
    â€“ Government employee (Pickering/Garcetti)
    â€“ Courtroom / legislative floor (non-public forum decorum)
    â€“ Prison/military (very deferential Turner/Oâ€™Brien balancing)
  4. If none of the above, apply ordinary forum analysis and strict scrutiny to content-based restrictions, intermediate to time-place-manner rules. level of scrutiny (3-5), forum (5 types), type of speech (true threat, incitement, fighting words, obscenity), two modes of challeneg, types of restrictions (as applied, subject matter, viewpoiint), level of protection (proteced, less-protected, unprotected)

Must track (1) category of speech, (2) type of restriction, (3) posture (facialâ€¯vsâ€¯asâ€‘applied)

Mnemonic Capsule

â€œCOHEN unless MILLER, BLACK, or BRANDENBURG takes it down, and FRASER or PACIFICA trims it in special places.â€

That single sentence encodes:
General protection (Cohen), lost only to the big categorical exclusions (Miller obscenity; Black true threats; Brandenburg incitement), and trimmed by special-venue carve-outs (Fraser schools; Pacifica broadcast).

1. United States v. Oâ€™Brien, 391 U.S. 367 (1968)

â€” Warren Court forges the four-part â€œOâ€™Brien testâ€ for laws that incidentally burden expressive conduct

Facts & Procedural Posture

  • Mar 31 1966 â†’ South Boston, MA: David Paul Oâ€™Brien, 19 years old, and three companions publicly burn their ==Selective-Service draft cards== on the steps of the federal courthouse before a crowd (including FBI agents).
  • Crowd attacks them; an agent brings Oâ€™Brien inside for safety. Oâ€™Brien admits purpose was to persuade others to resist the draft.
  • Charged under the 1965 amendment to Â§ 12(b)(3) of the Universal Military Training & Service Act (50 U.S.C. App. Â§ 462(b)(3)) â€” which, after 1965, ==criminalized knowingly destroying a registration certificate.==
  • U.S. District Court (D. Mass.) convicts; First Circuit affirms.
  • SCOTUS grants cert. Nov 13 1967; argued Jan 24 1968; decided May 27 1968.

Governmentâ€™s Theory

  • Draft cards are indispensable administrative tools; statute is ==content-neutral housekeeping==; disturbance to Selective-Service machinery justifies prohibition.

Oâ€™Brienâ€™s Theory

  1. Burning was ==symbolic speech protesting the war== â‡’ First Amendment shields the act.
  2. Statuteâ€™s ==â€œpurposeâ€ was to stifle anti-war expression== â‡’ unconstitutional content-based suppression.
  3. Law is ==overbroad/vague==; less restrictive alternatives exist (fine for failure to possess card, etc.).

Issues Presented

  1. Does the First Amendment protect destruction of draft cards as symbolic speech?
  2. What standard governs statutes that penalize conduct with both speech and non-speech elements?
  3. Was Â§ 462(b)(3) enacted to silence expression, rendering it unconstitutional?

Holding & Vote

  • 8 â€“ 1 (Warren C.J.; joined by Black, Harlan, Brennan, Stewart, White, Fortas, Marshall).
  • Conviction affirmed: statute is constitutional as applied.
  • Douglas dissents (absolutist view: speech + conduct inseparable; law suppresses dissent).

Core Reasoning (Warren, C.J.)

  1. Definitional move â€” Not every conduct labeled â€œspeechâ€ gets full immunity.
  2. Hybrid-conduct framework â€” When speech and non-speech elements are â€œcombined,â€ government may regulate the non-speech portion if four criteria are satisfied:

    (1) within constitutional power; (2) furthers an important/substantial governmental interest; (3) interest is unrelated to suppression of expression; (4) incidental restriction on First-Amendment freedoms is no greater than essential. â†’ The â€œOâ€™Brien test.â€

  3. Application
    • Congress has sweeping Art. I power to raise armies & administer Selective-Service.
    • Protecting cardsâ€™ availability is a substantial administrative interest.
    • Interest is content-neutral â€” ==law bars all destruction, pro- or anti-war.==
    • No narrower means will assure ready access to cards.
  4. Legislative motive â€” Even if some floor statements reflected hostility to dissent, valid broad purposes control absent proof of dominant censorship motive.

Douglas Dissent

  • Draft cards are â€œidentification papers of the kind ==so typical of totalitarian regimes.==â€
  • Burning is pure speech indistinguishable from uttered protest; majority trivializes expressive element.

Doctrinal Takeaways

  • Oâ€™Brien test = ==intermediate scrutiny for content-neutral laws regulating expressive conduct.==
  • Distinguishes from Texas v. Johnson category (content-based suppression) and from Miller obscenity.
  • Valid even where incidental burden on expression is foreseeable.
  • Legislative motive rarely fatal if statute is facially neutral.

Impact & Legacy

  • Forms backbone of symbolic-speech analysis (flag-desecration, nude-dancing, draft-card possession, mask bans).
  • Reappears in Ward v. Rock Against Racism (1989) & Turner Broad. v. FCC (1994) as template for tailoring inquiry.
  • Modern security-credential laws (airport IDs, badge destruction) routinely survive under Oâ€™Brien.

Socratic Traps & Answers

  • Q: Could Congress instead criminalize private flag-burning under Oâ€™Brien? â†’ No; ==purpose of reverence for the flag is content-related== â†’ strict scrutiny (Johnson).
  • Q: If Oâ€™Brien had worn the card as an armband? â†’ Mere display â‰  destruction â‡’ statute not triggered; would fall under pure speech (strict scrutiny for any content-based ban).
  • Q: Does Â§ 3 interest survive after the draft ends? â†’ Court defers to Congressâ€™s administrative judgment; future mootness irrelevant to 1968 conviction.

Mnemonic

O-B-R-I-E-N = Only Burden when Reasonable, Important, Expressive-Neutral, Narrow.

Bottom Line
Oâ€™Brien legitimizes content-neutral regulation of expressive conduct via the four-part test, anchoring modern intermediate scrutiny for symbolic-speech cases.

2. Tinker v. Des Moines I.C.S.D., 393 U.S. 503 (1969)

â€” Fortas opinion declares that students do not â€œshed their constitutional rights at the schoolhouse gate,â€ birthing the â€œmaterial-and-substantial disruptionâ€ test

  • Dec 1965 â†’ Des Moines, Iowa: siblings Mary Beth Tinker (13) & John Tinker (15) and friend Chris Eckhardt (16) ==plan to wear black armbands during Advent to mourn Vietnam war dead and advocate a truce.==
  • School principals adopt a pre-emptive rule: remove armband or face suspension until compliance.
  • Students wear bands; silently attend class; face peer comments but no violence. Five suspended.
  • Parents sue under 42 U.S.C. Â§ 1983; U.S. D.C. (S.D. Iowa) upholds school; Eighth Circuit affirms by ==equally divided en banc court.==
  • SCOTUS cert. granted Nov 12 1968; argued Nov 12; decided Feb 24 1969.

  • Armbands provoke controversy during wartime; forecast of disturbance justified preventive discipline; schools can prescribe dress and conduct.

Studentsâ€™ Position

  • ==Silent, passive== political expression = pure speech; no evidence of ==disruption==; policy singles out Vietnam opposition â‡’ ==viewpoint== discrimination.

  1. Are silent armbands in school protected speech under the First & Fourteenth Amendments?
  2. What standard governs school regulation of student expression?

  • 7 â€“ 2 (Fortas, Brennan, White, Marshall, Douglas, Stewart, Warren â‡¢ majority).
  • Discipline unconstitutional; suspensions reversed.
  • Black & Harlan dissent (Black joined by Harlan).

Core Reasoning (Fortas, J.)

  1. Students are â€œpersonsâ€ under the Constitution. ==They do not abandon rights at the schoolhouse gate.==
  2. Symbolic armband = â€œpure speech.â€ Historically, Barnette flag-salute and Meyer language-teaching protect student/teacher expression.
  3. Standard articulated â€” School may regulate only where expression would ==â€œmaterially and substantially disruptâ€== classwork or invade othersâ€™ rights; mere ==â€œundifferentiated fearâ€== or desire to avoid discomfort is insufficient.
  4. Application â€” Record shows no disruption; policy singled out Vietnam armbands while allowing other political symbols â‡’ viewpoint discrimination.
  5. Tolerance of controversy is essential to democratic education; suppression cultivates â€œtotalitarian enclaves.â€

  • Predicts erosion of discipline; courts usurp local control; students are in school to learn, not teach; any deviation distracts.

  • â€œTinker testâ€: speech protected unless school proves ==material/substantial disruption or invasion of rights.==
  • Distinguishes later Fraser (lewd speech), Hazelwood (school-sponsored curriculum), Morse (drug advocacy), Mahanoy (off-campus).

  • Backbone of student-speech jurisprudence; cited in social-media cases, LGBTQ armbands, climate-strike walkouts, Black Lives Matter shirts.
  • Post-Columbine & Parkland walkouts reignited debates over â€œdisruption.â€
  • International influence: echoed in Canadian, Indian, and European education-rights rulings.

Socratic Traps & Answers

  • Q: Would a walkout be protected? â†’ Likely disruptive â†’ school may discipline under Tinker.
  • Q: Confederate-flag shirts? â†’ Evaluate disruption & harassment of protected classes; many circuits uphold bans if racial tension evidence exists.
  • Q: Off-campus Snapchat rant? â†’ Mahanoy: schools have reduced authority; must prove substantial disruption with greater burden.

â€œTINKER = Teachers Impose? No, Kidsâ€™ Expression Remainsâ€”unless real disruption.â€

Bottom Line

3. Texas v. Johnson, 491 U.S. 397 (1989)

â€” Rehnquist Court holds that flag-burning is core political expression, striking down content-based bans

  • Aug 22 1984 â†’ Dallas, TX: During the Republican National Convention, protester Gregory Lee Johnson douses a U.S. flag in kerosene and burns it while chanting political slogans (â€œReagan, Mondale, the same oldâ€¦â€) as demonstrators march past City Hall.
  • Some onlookers are offended; no violence ensues.
  • Johnson arrested under Tex. Pen. Code Â§ 42.09(a)(3) (â€œ==desecration of a venerated object in a way that seriously offends observers==â€).
  • Trial court convicts; sentenced to 1 year + $2,000 fine.
  • Texas Court of Appeals affirms; ==Texas Court of Criminal Appeals reverses==, holding statute unconstitutional.
  • SCOTUS grants cert. Jan 1988; argued Mar 21 1989; decision June 21 1989.

Texasâ€™s Theory

  1. State possesses a â€œunique interestâ€ in preserving the flag as a symbol of nationhood and unity.
  2. Statute addresses â€œnon-speechâ€ conduct (fire), or at worst is content-neutral because it punishes the mode of desecration, not viewpoint.

Johnsonâ€™s Theory

  1. Flag-burning is expressive conduct squarely within the First Amendment.
  2. Statute is content-basedâ€”offense hinges on audience reaction (â€œseriously offendsâ€), triggering strict scrutiny.
  3. No compelling interest justifies silencing political protest.

Issues Presented

  1. Is flag burning expressive conduct protected by the First Amendment?
  2. If so, does Texasâ€™s interest in preserving the flagâ€™s symbolic value justify a content-based restriction?

Holding & Vote

  • 5 â€“ 4 (Brennan, J.; joined by Marshall, Blackmun, Scalia, Kennedy).
  • Statute unconstitutional; conviction reversed.
  • Dissents: Rehnquist (C.J., joined by White & Oâ€™Connor) and Stevens.

Core Reasoning (Brennan)

  1. Expressive nature concededâ€”the State stipulated Johnsonâ€™s act was â€œpolitical.â€
  2. Oâ€™Brien inapplicableâ€”==law is content-based==: whether burning is criminal depends on the message observers perceive (==offense==). Strict scrutiny applies.
  3. No compelling interest
    • Preserving the flag as a symbol = interest in suppressing a particular message; the very function of the First Amendment is to forbid government from controlling symbolsâ€™ meaning.
    • Preventing breaches of the peace is speculative; actual disturbance did not occur and is addressable with breach-of-peace statutes.
  4. Bedrock principle: Government may not prohibit expression simply because society finds the idea offensive or disagreeable.
  5. No separate category of the national flag

  • Flag is a singular national emblem; long tradition of special protection.
  • Johnsonâ€™s act is conduct with minimal â€œspeechâ€ value; Oâ€™Brien intermediate scrutiny satisfied.

  • The ==flag embodies ideals transcending verbal expression==; burning destroys that unique communicative resource and can be banned without stifling Johnsonâ€™s political message.

  • Content-based vandalism statutes aimed at a symbolâ€™s communicative impact trigger strict scrutiny.
  • â€œOffenseâ€ to viewers is insufficient justification.
  • Decision later extended in United States v. Eichman (1990), striking federal Flag Protection Act.

  • Codified the right to desecrate flags as political protest; recurring congressional attempts at flag-desecration amendments fail.
  • Forms modern template for analyzing viewpoint-based vandalism laws targeting other symbols (crosses, rainbow flags).

Socratic Traps & Answers

  • Q: Could Texas ban all open-flame demonstrations for fire safety? â†’ Yes, if content-neutral and narrowly tailored (time-place-manner).
  • Q: What if Johnson stole the flag? â†’ Theft laws apply; First Amendment no defense to independent unlawful conduct.
  • Q: Can Congress amend the Constitution to forbid flag burning? â†’ Yes; constitutional amendment supersedes Johnson.

â€œJOHNSON burns, STATE churns â€” but offense alone canâ€™t quench free speech.â€

Bottom Line

4. Ward v. Rock Against Racism, 491 U.S. 781 (1989)

â€” Refines time-, place-, manner doctrine; â€œnarrow tailoringâ€ â‰  â€œleast-restrictive meansâ€ for content-neutral regulations

  • Central Park, NYC: Since 1967, Naumburg Bandshell hosts summer concerts. Nearby residents complain of excessive volume from rock shows promoted by Rock Against Racism (RAR).
  • 1986: NYC Parks Department issues a regulation requiring bandshell performers to use city-provided sound-amplification equipment and a city-employed sound technician.
  • RAR sues (42 U.S.C. Â§ 1983), claiming the regulation chills artistic expression.
  • S.D.N.Y. upholds rule; Second Circuit reverses (not least-restrictive means).
  • SCOTUS cert. Nov 1988; argued Feb 27 1989; decision June 22 1989.

Cityâ€™s Theory

  • Regulation is content-neutral; primary purpose = control volume, protect residentsâ€™ tranquility; expertise of technician ensures uniform compliance.

RARâ€™s Theory

  1. Mandated city technician gives officials content control (mute unpopular speech).
  2. Even if content-neutral, regulation isnâ€™t narrowly tailoredâ€”city could use decibel limits or post-event penalties (â€œleast-restrictive meansâ€).

Issues Presented

  1. Is the sound-equipment rule content-neutral?
  2. What constitutes â€œnarrow tailoringâ€ for a content-neutral time-, place-, manner restriction?

  • 6 â€“ 3 (Kennedy, J.; joined by Rehnquist (C.J.), White, Stevens, Oâ€™Connor, Scalia).
  • Regulation constitutional; Second Circuit reversed.
  • Dissents: Brennan (joined by Marshall), Blackmun separately.

Core Reasoning (Kennedy)

  1. Content neutralityâ€”Rule applies regardless of music genre or viewpoint; decision to require city equipment is unrelated to message conveyed.
  2. Time-, place-, manner framework (from Clark v. CCNV):
    • Significant governmental interest â†’ safeguarding residential quiet and park quality.
    • Narrow tailoring â†’ does not require the least-restrictive means; law must simply not burden substantially more speech than necessary to further the interest.
    • Ample alternative channels â†’ Bands may perform, choose repertoire, control volume (through requested settings), or play elsewhere.
  3. Cityâ€™s centralized technician is a reasonable fit; alternatives (post-hoc fines, equipment confiscation) less effective.

  • Mandated technician risks content manipulation; decibel limits sufficeâ€”city chose a course that unnecessarily intrudes on artistic control, violating â€œnarrow tailoring.â€

  • Questions factual predicate; no proof cityâ€™s own technicians outperform private ones; remand for findings.

Doctrinal Takeaways

  • Clarifies that â€œnarrow tailoringâ€ in a content-neutral context â‰  strict-scrutiny â€œleast-restrictive means.â€
  • Reinforces Oâ€™Brien / Clark line: incidental speech burdens okay if reasonable fit.
  • Validates government management of public-forum logisticsâ€”sound, safety, permit capsâ€”if message-neutral.

Impact & Legacy

  • Cited in noise-ordinance and permit-fee cases; provides template for regulating protest amplification (McCullen v. Coakley cites Ward).
  • Influences city-busking rules, parade amplification, nightclub zoning.

Socratic Traps & Answers

  • Q: Could NYC limit concerts to <90 dB without specifying equipment? â†’ Yes; still content-neutral.
  • Q: What if technician turned down volume only for political speeches he disliked? â†’ As-applied First-Amendment violation.
  • Q: Does â€œnot least-restrictiveâ€ rule apply in strict-scrutiny settings (e.g., content-based)? â†’ No; strict scrutiny still demands least-restrictive means.

Mnemonic

â€œWARD: We Arenâ€™t Required to deliver the Least deviceâ€”just a Reasonably tailored one.â€

Bottom Line
Content-neutral regulations need only be reasonablyâ€”not minimallyâ€”restrictive; government may impose uniform technical constraints (like sound systems) to advance significant interests without micromanaging message content.

Supreme Court Case Rule / Test Clarified Speech Unprotected / Regulated Sphere of Application
Texas v. Johnson (1989) Strict scrutiny for content-based bans; offense is no justification Flag-burning criminalization struck down Viewpoint-targeted vandalism laws
Ward v. Rock Against Racism (1989) Content-neutral time-place-manner: â€œnarrow tailoring â‰  least-restrictive meansâ€ City-mandated sound system upheld Forum management (noise, permits, logistics)

When evaluating whether a law that restricts expressive conduct violates the First Amendment, courts ask whether the regulation is content-based or content-neutral. The Selective Service card-burning case (United States v. O'Brien, 1968) is the paradigm for content-neutral regulation of conduct. The law didnâ€™t care why you burned your draft cardâ€”it prohibited destruction of the card to maintain the functionality of the Selective Service system. This purpose was unrelated to the message expressed, so the regulation was upheld under what became known as the Oâ€™Brien test, which permits content-neutral restrictions on expressive conduct if: (1) the regulation is within the governmentâ€™s constitutional power, (2) it furthers an important or substantial governmental interest, (3) the interest is unrelated to the suppression of expression, and (4) the restriction is no greater than necessary to further that interest.

By contrast, flag-burning prohibitions that punish only protest-based or offensive uses, as in Texas v. Johnson (1989), are content-based. The law at issue didnâ€™t ban all flag burningâ€”it criminalized expressive flag desecration, i.e., burning with the intent to criticize or offend. This meant the stateâ€™s action depended on what the speaker was trying to sayâ€”a textbook violation of viewpoint neutrality and a trigger for strict scrutiny, which the law failed.

In Morse v. Frederick (2007), the Court allowed a school to discipline a student for unfurling a â€œBONG HiTS 4 JESUSâ€ banner at a school event. While the decision acknowledged that student speech is protected under Tinker, it carved out an exception for speech seen as promoting illegal drug use. The ruling was widely criticized for being content-based, though the Court tried to frame it as a school-based regulation of illegal advocacy, not viewpoint discrimination. It shows how context and institutional settingâ€”here, the school environmentâ€”can sometimes skew doctrinal application.

Turning to forum doctrine, International Society for Krishna Consciousness v. Lee (1992) addressed airport terminals, where the Court held that solicitation could be banned because the space was a nonpublic forum, not traditionally open to expressive activity. However, it did allow distribution of literature, highlighting the subtlety of permissible regulation in limited spaces. The test here focuses on reasonableness and viewpoint neutrality, not strict scrutiny, because the government has more control over nontraditional forums.

In Burson v. Freeman (1992), the Court upheld a law banning electioneering within 100 feet of polling places. Although the law was content-basedâ€”targeting political speechâ€”it survived strict scrutiny due to the compelling state interest in protecting electoral integrity and voter privacy. This case is an example of content-based speech restrictions that can survive, but only when the justification is compelling and the law is narrowly tailored.

Lastly, obscenity law remains an outlier. In Roth v. United States (1957), the Court first held that obscenity is not protected speech under the First Amendment. This was later refined by the infamous plurality in Jacobellis v. Ohio (1964), where Justice Stewart declined to define hardcore pornography but famously said, â€œI know it when I see it.â€ That phrase encapsulates the subjectivity and instability of obscenity doctrine, which has often invited criticism for its moralistic and inconsistent enforcement, though it remains technically valid law under the Miller test today.

Together, these cases illuminate the delicateâ€”and often contradictoryâ€”line the Court tries to draw between conduct and expression, protected and unprotected categories, and the forum-based flexibility of speech rights. The critical inquiry always turns on the same few pivots: is the law regulating the message or the medium, and is the restriction justified independent of suppressing expression? The more a law relies on assessing the meaning behind a speakerâ€™s act, the more likely it is to run afoul of the First Amendment.

Week 8.1

1. West Virginia State Board of Education v. Barnette, 319 U.S. 624 (1943)*

â€” Jackson Court buries Gobitis, establishes the modern doctrine of compelled-speech and the â€œfixed-starâ€ principle

  • The user wrote â€œ1948â€; the landmark decision was handed down June 14 1943, Flag Day.

Facts & Procedural Posture

  • After Minersville v. Gobitis (1940): West Virginia amended its statutes to mandate courses in patriotism andâ€”by Board resolution (Jan 9 1942)â€”a daily flag-salute for all public-school pupils. Non-compliance = â€œinsubordination,â€ punished by expulsion; truancy statutes exposed parents to $50 fine / 30 days jail.
  • Jehovahâ€™s Witnesses (Barnette family et al.) refuse on biblical grounds (Exodus 20:4-5). Children expelled; parents threatened with delinquency charges.
  • Suit in U.S. District Court (S.D. W.Va.) seeks injunction under 42 U.S.C. Â§ 1983. Three-judge court grants relief; state appeals directly to SCOTUS.

Stateâ€™s Theory

  1. National-unity imperative: Flag salute vital to inculcate civic cohesion (borrowing Gobitis language).
  2. Salute is conduct, not protected speech; even if speech, school officials may set curriculum prerequisites.

Barnetteâ€™s Theory

  1. Flag salute is symbolic expression; compelled affirmation violates both speech and free-exercise guarantees.
  2. Regulation is viewpoint-specificâ€”government dictating orthodox belief.
  3. No â€œclear & present dangerâ€ from silent non-participants.

Issues Presented

  1. Does the First Amendment forbid states from compelling students to salute the flag and recite the Pledge as a condition of attending public school?
  2. Does the interest in national unity justify compulsion?

Holding & Vote

  • 6 â€“ 3. Jackson, J. (Stone C.J., Black, Douglas, Murphy, Roberts concurring) reverses; mandatory salute unconstitutional.
  • Dissent: Frankfurter, J. (joined by Roberts & Reed).

Core Reasoning (Jackson)

  1. Speech vs. Conduct â€“ A salute is â€œa form of utterance. Symbolism is a primitive but effective way of communicating ideas.â€
  2. Compulsion > Suppression â€“ Forcing affirmation intrudes more deeply than silencing dissent; no =="clear and present danger"== justifies it.
  3. Fixed-Star Maxim â€“ â€œIf there is any fixed star in our constitutional constellation, it is that no official â€¦ can prescribe what shall be orthodox in politics, nationalism, religion, or other matters of opinion.â€
  4. Majoritarianism rejected â€“ Rights are withdrawn â€œfrom the vicissitudes of political controversy.â€
  5. Overrules Gobitis â€“ Courts, not transient legislatures, guard fundamental liberties.

Frankfurter Dissent (highlights)

  • Judicial restraint: ==curriculum choices lie with elected bodies;== Court improperly substitutes its judgment.
  • Predicts civic disorder if ==courts become â€œschool boards.â€==

Doctrinal Takeaways

  • Compelled-Speech Doctrine: Government may not force affirmation of belief.
  • Introduces modern symbolic-speech analysis (later used in Wooley, Hurley, Janus).
  • Distinguishes instruction (permissible) from confession of faith (impermissible).

Impact & Legacy

  • Flag-salute mandates nationwide invalidated.
  • Serves as cornerstone for refusals to display messages (Wooley), to fund speech (Pacific Gas), and to join unions (Janus).
  • Elevated Justice Jacksonâ€™s reputation for eloquent civil-liberties prose.

Socratic Traps & Answers

  • Q: Could a voluntary patriotic ceremony be required for extracurricular participation?
    A: Yesâ€”voluntary participation avoids coercion; state just canâ€™t compel attendance or expression.
  • Q: May schools teach the history and meaning of the flag?
    A: Absolutely; informational instruction is content-neutral pedagogy.
  • Q: Could the state punish students for disrupting peersâ€™ salute?
    A: Yesâ€”schools may regulate disruptive conduct without policing viewpoints.

Mnemonic

**â€œBARâ€“NETTE bars the Bannerâ€™s Affirmationâ€”Rights Neither Ebb _n_or Travel to Tyrannyâ€™s Edict.â€

Bottom Line The First Amendment forbids compulsory patriotism; government cannot command citizensâ€”least of all schoolchildrenâ€”to mouth an orthodox creed.

2. Wooley v. Maynard, 430 U.S. 705 (1977)

â€” Burger Court extends Barnette: the right to refrain from displaying a stateâ€™s ideological motto on private property

Facts & Procedural Posture

  • New Hampshire statute (1969): All passenger-car plates must bear state motto â€œLIVE FREE OR DIE.â€
  • George & Maxine Maynard (Jehovahâ€™s Witnesses) tape over / trim off motto; cited thrice under Â§ 262:27-c (misdemeanor for obscuring plate).
    • Convicted â†’ $25 & $50 fines + 15 days jail (served).
  • File Â§ 1983 suit; three-judge U.S. District Court enjoins enforcement; state appeals.

Stateâ€™s Theory

  1. Vehicle-identification: Motto aids police in spotting proper passenger plates.
  2. Civic-patriotic interest: Promotes â€œhistory, individualism, state pride.â€

*Maynardsâ€™ Theory

  1. Forced display makes their car a â€œmobile billboardâ€ for an abhorrent slogan.
  2. First Amendment protects both speech and silenceâ€”right to refuse to propagate an idea.
  3. Less-restrictive alternatives (unique plate colors, numbers) meet identification goal.

Issues Presented

  1. Does compelling motorists to display an ideological motto violate the First Amendment right not to speak?
  2. Are New Hampshireâ€™s interests sufficiently compelling and narrowly tailored?

Holding & Vote

  • 6 â€“ 3 (Burger, C.J.; Brennan, Stewart, White, Marshall, Powell, Stevens).
  • Statute unconstitutional as applied; injunction affirmed.
  • Dissent: Rehnquist, J. (joined by Blackmun). (Court membership in 1977 makes this effectively a 7-2 decision; ==Justice While== joined both the dissent and the majority.)

Core Reasoning (Burger)

  1. Complementary freedoms â€“ â€œThe right to speak and the right to refrain from speaking are â€¦ complementary components of â€˜individual freedom of mind.â€™â€
  2. No genuine ID need â€“ Numeric/letter configurations already distinguish passenger plates; motto adds nothing.
  3. Ideological dissemination â€“ Stateâ€™s second aim is non-neutral; government may not draft citizens as involuntary couriers of ideology.
  4. Narrow-tailoring requirement â€“ Even substantial ends cannot be pursued by broadly stifling personal liberties when narrower means suffice (citing Shelton v. Tucker).

Rehnquist Dissent (with Blackmun)

  • No â€œspeechâ€ implicated; plate understood as stateâ€™s utterance, not driverâ€™s.
  • Citizens free to add bumper stickers disclaiming motto; hence no compelled affirmation.

Doctrinal Takeaways

  • Barnette + Oâ€™Brien fusion: Where government commandeers private property to display its message, strict scrutiny applies.
  • Distinguishes government speech (currencyâ€™s â€œIn God We Trustâ€)â€”portable objects not uniquely tied to a single speaker.
  • Lays groundwork for Hurley, Johanns, Janus on compelled subsidies or messages.

Impact & Legacy

  • Plates now offer â€œalternative mottoâ€ or sticker options in several states.
  • Cited in cases striking compelled slogans on mission statements, union fees, and specialty plates.

Socratic Traps & Answers

  • Q: Could NH let drivers opt-out by paying extra for a plain plate?
    A: Probably constitutionalâ€”compulsion removed; fee must be nominal to avoid penalty-in-disguise.
  • Q: What about â€œIn God We Trustâ€ on currency?
    A: Court hints difference: bills arenâ€™t publicly affixed to the bearer and are clearly state speech.
  • Q: Could the state mandate a safety slogan (â€œBuckle Up!â€) on plates?
    A: If purely factual, non-ideological, and not contested belief, lower scrutiny may apply; but compelled ideological speech remains forbidden.

Mnemonic

â€œWOOLEY wonâ€™t wear the mottoâ€”Witnesses Obscure Obligatory Line; Expression _Y_ours, not Stateâ€™s.â€

Bottom Line The First Amendment shields individuals from being forced to carry the stateâ€™s ideological messages on their own property; silence, too, is protected speech.

3. Pleasant Grove City v. Summum, 555 U.S. 460 (2009)

â€” Roberts Court crystallizes the modern government-speech doctrine: permanent park monuments speak for the sovereign, not for passing citizens

Facts & Procedural Posture

  • Pioneer Park, Pleasant Grove, UT: a 2.5-acre city park already displaying fifteen permanent exhibitsâ€”including a Ten Commandments slab donated by the Fraternal Order of Eagles in 1971â€”became the focus of dispute when the religious group Summum twice (2003) petitioned to install its own stone monument espousing the â€œSeven Aphorisms of Summum.â€ The city, invoking unwritten custom limiting monuments to items germane to local history or long-standing community ties, refused.
  • A formal policy codifying those criteria followed in 2004; a third Summum request in 2005 was likewise denied.
  • Summum sued under 42 U.S.C. Â§ 1983. The District Court sided with Pleasant Grove; the Tenth Circuit reversedâ€”treating the park as a traditional public forum and the cityâ€™s refusal as impermissible viewpoint discrimination. SCOTUS granted certiorari.

Petitionersâ€™ (Cityâ€™s) Theory

  1. Permanent monuments are government expression. Acceptance constitutes the cityâ€™s own message, hence immune from Free Speech Clause constraints.
  2. Even if forum principles applied, physical scarcity and aesthetic curation justify selective acceptance; strict neutrality would force either chaos or closure.

Respondentâ€™s (Summumâ€™s) Theory

  1. Public parks are per se traditional public fora; refusing its monument while retaining others is viewpoint discrimination.
  2. The city could satisfy equal-treatment obligations through neutral criteria (e.g., size or safety) rather than content-laden judgments.

Issues Presented

  1. Does the Free Speech Clause require a municipality to accept a privately donated permanent monument when it has accepted others?
  2. Are permanent monuments in public parks subject to public-forum analysisâ€”or are they instances of government speech unregulated by the Clause?

Holding & Vote

  • 9â€“0 (Alito, J.): Reversed. The display of a permanent monument in a city park is government speech; therefore, the Free Speech Clause imposes no viewpoint-neutrality obligation on the city.

Core Reasoning (Alito)

  1. Historical practice of selective receptivity. Governments have long chosen, financed, or accepted monuments to craft civic identityâ€”Statue of Liberty, Iwo Jima, Vietnam Wall, etc.
  2. Attribution logic. Observers reasonably associate an installed monument with the landownerâ€™s voice; selective placement and perpetual presence convey an official message.
  3. Forum doctrine â€œout of place.â€ Unlike leafleting or rallies (transitory and multitudinous), land cannot host limitless stone structures; compelling the city to display every offered monument would either clutter parks or chill acceptance altogether.
  4. Limits remain. Government speech must still respect other constitutional commands (e.g., Establishment Clause), but is not cabined by the Free Speech Clause.

Concurring Perspectives (Highlights)

  • Roberts, C.J. (joined by Scalia & Thomas): Emphasizes that the Free Speech Clause â€œis a limit on government, not a limit on itself.â€
  • Souter, J.: Flags future need to distinguish government speech from government funding of private speech.
  • Breyer, J.: Suggests a functional, not formal, approachâ€”looking to context and likely attribution.

Doctrinal Takeaways

  • Establishes a bright-line rule: permanent monuments accepted for display on government land are government speech; forum analysis (public, designated, limited) is irrelevant.
  • Fortifies the government-speech shield later invoked in Walker v. Texas Division, Sons of Confederate Veterans (2015) to uphold state refusal of specialty plates bearing Confederate symbols.
  • Signals that when the state speaks, it may curate content, though that power is tempered by other constitutional guarantees (e.g., Establishment Clause scrutiny of Ten Commandments displays).

Impact & Legacy

  • Cities nationwide cite Summum to defend selective monument policies, art-commission vetoes, and removal or retention of controversial statuary.
  • Invigorates scholarly debate over the â€œdemocratic accountabilityâ€ rationale for shielding government speech from First-Amendment challenge.

Socratic Traps & Answers

  • Q: Could Summum insist merely on staging an annual ceremony beside the Ten Commandments?
    A: Yesâ€”temporary oral speech remains private expression in a public forum, subject to standard time-place-manner rules.
  • Q: Does Summum license cities to censor hostile leaflets in parks?
    A: Noâ€”the ruling is confined to permanent structures clearly attributed to the government; transient private speech still enjoys forum protection.
  • Q: Must the city remove its Ten Commandments slab after Summum if it fears an Establishment-Clause suit?
    A: Possiblyâ€”Summum offers no immunity from Establishment scrutiny; government speech can still violate the Religion Clauses.

Mnemonic

â€œSUMMUM stones = State tonesâ€”park monuments speak for the polity, not the passer-by.â€

Bottom Line A permanent monument in a city park belongs to the governmentâ€™s voice; the Free Speech Clause does not obligate equal access for competing messages, though other constitutional constraints may still apply.

Q3: Is government speech truly unregulated?

â€œCan the government say anything as long as it doesnâ€™t abridge rights or promote religion?â€

  • Short answer: Mostly yes, but with important caveats.

Government Speech Doctrine

  • The government can speak freely in its official capacity. It's not bound by viewpoint neutrality like private speech forums.
  • Walker v. Texas Division, Sons of Confederate Veterans (2015) â†’ License plates as government speech, can exclude Confederate flag.
  • Pleasant Grove City v. Summum (2009) â†’ City can accept a Ten Commandments monument but reject others on gov property.

Limits on Government Speech

  1. Establishment Clause â€“ Gov speech cannot promote religion, especially in legislative or school settings (e.g., Town of Greece v. Galloway vs Lee v. Weisman).
  2. Compelled Speech â€“ If gov forces others to carry its message, then itâ€™s not just gov speech anymore (e.g., Wooley v. Maynard).
  3. Civil Rights Context â€“ Speech by officials (esp. federal employees) can trigger Â§1983 or Bivens liability if it creates a hostile environment or infringes protected rights. Not all speech is shielded under â€œgovernment functionâ€ if it's discriminatory or retaliatory.

â†’ Still, this is a high bar. Most civil suits are against conduct, not speech.

Ceballos v. Garcetti (2006)

  • Public employee speech case.
  • If gov employee speaks pursuant to official duties, their speech is not protected under the First Amendment.
  • Reinforces gov speech doctrineâ€”employer control over internal messaging is broad.

â†’ Think about the state as employer vs. the state as sovereign legislator.

Stone in Gobitis vs. Carolene Products

  • Minersville School District v. Gobitis (1940):
    • Justice Stone dissented from majority that upheld mandatory flag salute.
    • Precursor to West Virginia v. Barnette (1943), which overruled it on compelled speech grounds.
  • Carolene Products (1938):
    • Footnote 4 (Stone again): Birth of â€œdiscrete and insular minoritiesâ€ doctrine. Suggests strict scrutiny might be appropriate where laws affect fundamental rights or marginalized groups.

â†’ Stone emerges as a proto-civil libertarian voice across different domains (economic regulation vs civil liberty).

ğŸ†” Oklahoma â€“ Sex Offender Label on ID

  • Constitutional Issue: Compelled speech vs Government speech.
  • If the label is part of a general state ID system, the state might argue itâ€™s government speech, intended for public safety.

BUTâ€¦

  • Courts often apply strict scrutiny if it:
