ğŸ“Œ Summary Principles

  • Government Speech = Generally unregulated, but:
    • Must avoid Establishment Clause violations.
    • Cannot compel individuals to express ideology.
    • Speech by employees or officials can face restriction or liability if:
      • Within employment context (Garcetti);
      • Discriminatory or violating civil rights.
  • Compelled Speech = Strict scrutiny applies.
    • Must be narrowly tailored to a compelling interest.
    • Even â€œsymbolicâ€ speech (ID labels, slogans, flags) is protected.
  • Stoneâ€™s influence:
    • Anticipates liberty-conscious dissent in both civil liberties and fundamental rights jurisprudence.

Week 8 Discussion

==General Rule: Viewpoint Discrimination==

"Really hard to argue in favor of viewpoint-based restrictions."

Key Doctrine:

  • Strict Scrutiny applies to viewpoint discrimination.
  • Government cannot favor or disfavor particular perspectives in:
    • Traditional public forums
    • Designated public forums
    • Limited public forums (if within subject matter)

Takeaway:

If a law or regulation targets the specific opinion or perspective being expressed, it is almost always unconstitutional.

â†’ See: Rosenberger v. UVA (1995), Lambâ€™s Chapel (1993), and Reed v. Town of Gilbert (2015).

Hypo 2: Ban on Indoor Fires, Except for Chemistry

Policy bans all indoor open flames except for chemistry labs.

Facial Challenge:

  • Facially content-neutral â†’ rule applies to all "fire expression" equally.
  • But potentially underinclusive â†’ why are chemistry labs privileged? Could imply speaker identity discrimination (if students in art or religion canâ€™t use candles, etc.).

As-Applied Challenge:

  • If someone is disciplined for lighting a memorial candle, or an art installation â†’ this could be:
    • Underinclusive if it only permits fires for academic disciplines the school prefers.
    • A possible free expression or free exercise issue, depending on context.

Doctrinal Framing:

  • Is the rule truly content-neutral or content-based under the surface?
  • Does the exception for chemistry create an arbitrary or discriminatory preference?
  • Does it trigger a forum analysis (e.g., classroom as a limited public forum)?

Hypo 3: Public Park Weight Restriction

Law bans carrying any sign over 5lbs in a public park during mass gatherings.

Forum Type:

  • Public Park = Traditional Public Forum
    • Government has least leeway to restrict speech.
    • Can only enforce narrowly tailored, content-neutral time, place, and manner regulations.

Content-Neutral or Not?

  • On its face, the rule appears content-neutral â†’ weight-based, not idea-based.
  • BUT if it disproportionately affects protest signs, or is enforced selectively, could be:
    • Pretext for content suppression
    • Subject to heightened scrutiny

Government Interest:

  • Claimed interest: prevent damage or injury (e.g., from heavy signs falling).
    • May be legitimate, but the rationale must be logical:
      • Is a falling sign only dangerous when carried?
      • What about heavy equipment used for non-expressive purposes?

Narrow Tailoring?

  • Is the 5lb limit the least restrictive means?
  • Are there alternative channels of communication?
  • Are non-expressive activities (e.g., sports gear, chairs) regulated similarly?

ğŸ› ï¸ Alternative Test: CN-NT-Alt

Element Test
Content-Neutral Rule does not target message or viewpoint
Narrowly Tailored Cannot burden more speech than necessary
Ample Alternatives Speakers must retain effective communication options

â†’ See: Ward v. Rock Against Racism (1989)

Student Speech Standard

â€œMaterially and substantially disrupt classwork or invade othersâ€™ rightsâ€; mere undifferentiated fear or discomfort is not enough.

Standard from:

  • Tinker v. Des Moines (1969)

Whatâ€™s Not Enough:

  • Avoiding controversy
  • Offense or disagreement
  • Desire for administrative ease

â†’ To restrict student expression, schools must demonstrate actual or reasonably forecasted disruption to educational function.

Compelled Speech Core Principle

"Looking for an element of compulsion."

Key Question:

  • Is the government merely speaking for itself (govâ€™t speech)?
  • Or is the government forcing an individual to express or endorse a message?

Key Tests:

Doctrine Case(s) Test / Threshold
Compelled Speech Wooley v. Maynard (1977) Forced to carry ideological message against will
Employee Speech Regulation Garcetti v. Ceballos (2006) If speech is part of official duties, it's not protected
Government Speech Walker v. Sons of Confederate Veterans If reasonable observer sees it as govâ€™t position â†’ no individual rights

Final Takeaways

  • First Amendment cases live and die on classifications:
    • Forum (traditional / designated / limited / nonpublic)
    • Nature of speech (content-neutral vs viewpoint)
    • Speaker (citizen, student, employee, government)
    • Regulatory aim (avoiding disruption, safety, government identity, etc.)
  • Always walk through:
    1. What kind of speech is this?
    2. Who is the speaker?
    3. What is the forum?
    4. What is the regulationâ€™s purpose?
    5. Is it content-based or viewpoint-based?
    6. Does it compel expression or suppress dissent?

Week 8.2 -- Libel and Defamation

1. New York Times Co. v. Sullivan, 376 U.S. 254 (1964)

â€” Warren Court constitutionalizes American libel law: only â€œactual maliceâ€ can silence criticism of public officials

Facts & Procedural Posture

  • Montgomery, AL (1960): A full-page advertisement, â€œHeed Their Rising Voices,â€ ran in The New York Times, soliciting funds for the civil-rights struggle and recounting police abuses. Several factual details were inaccurate (e.g., students sang the wrong anthem; King arrested four, not seven, times).
  • L. B. Sullivan, elected Commissioner of Public Affairs (in charge of police), claimed the word â€œpoliceâ€ implicitly libeled him. Alabama law treated such words as libel per seâ€”injury, falsity, and malice presumed.
  • State jury awarded Sullivan $500,000 against the Times and four Black clergy signatories; Alabama Supreme Court affirmed. Cert granted to decide how far the First and Fourteenth Amendments limit state libel verdicts.

Petitionersâ€™ (Times & Clergy) Theory

  1. Criticism of official conduct on public issues lies at the First Amendmentâ€™s core; l==iability without fault== yields unconstitutional self-censorship.
  2. Alabamaâ€™s doctrine of â€œpresumed maliceâ€ inverts the burden of proof, chilling debate.
  3. Advertisement did not refer to Sullivan â€œof and concerningâ€ him; verdict lacks evidentiary basis.

Respondentâ€™s (Sullivan) Theory

  1. Libelous speech sits outside constitutional protection; states may set strict liability to vindicate reputational interests.
  2. Errors of fact, even in political ads, forfeit First Amendment immunity.
  3. Jury reasonably inferred he was targeted as the police commissioner.

Issues Presented

  1. What constitutional standard governs defamation suits by public officials over speech about their official conduct?
  2. Does the First Amendment permit a verdict resting on presumed falsity and malice, with the burden on defendants to prove truth?
  3. Were the challenged statements â€œof and concerningâ€ Commissioner Sullivan?

Holding & Vote

  • 9 â€“ 0 (Brennan, J.): A public official may recover for defamatory falsehood relating to official conduct only by proving â€œactual maliceâ€â€”knowledge of falsity or reckless disregard for truthâ€”by â€œclear and convincingâ€ evidence. Alabama verdict reversed.

Core Reasoning (Brennan)

  1. Robust public debate imperative. Democracy demands â€œuninhibited, robust, wide-openâ€ criticism of officialsâ€”even â€œvehement, causticâ€ attacks.
  2. Error is inevitable in free discussion; compelling critics to guarantee truth on pain of ruin chills speech.
  3. Actual-malice rule balances free debate with reputational interests, preserving breathing space.
  4. Presumptions of falsity/malice and strict liability conflict with the First Amendment; evidence also insufficient to show the ad was â€œof and concerningâ€ Sullivan.

Concurring Perspectives (Highlights)

  • Black & Douglas: First Amendment is absoluteâ€”government may never punish criticism of officials, true or false.
  • Goldberg (joined by Douglas & Brennan): â€œActual maliceâ€ applies to all speech on public issues, not merely attacks on officials.

Doctrinal Takeaways

  • Birth of the â€œpublic-official actual-maliceâ€ test, later extended to public figures (Curtis Publishing v. Butts, 1967) and limited-purpose figures.
  • Shifts libel from strict state tort to constitutional domain; states may not impose presumed damages or punitive awards absent actual malice.
  • Establishes clear-and-convincing-evidence standard of proof.

Impact & Legacy

  • Transformed U.S. press freedom; encouraged investigative journalism on Vietnam, Watergate, etc.
  • Continues to shield media (and today, bloggers & social media users) when criticizing officials, though doctrine now challenged by some Justices and legislators.

Socratic Traps & Answers

  • Q: If a mayor sues a local blog for a small factual mistake, does Sullivan apply?
    A: Yesâ€”mayor is a public official; plaintiff must show actual malice.
  • Q: Does Sullivan protect knowing lies about a private individual?
    A: Noâ€”private plaintiffs need show only negligence (Gertz v. Robert Welch, 1974).
  • Q: Can a state flip the burden, requiring defendants to prove lack of malice?
    A: Noâ€”the First Amendment requires the plaintiff to prove presence of actual malice.

Mnemonic

â€œSULLIVAN shields scrutinyâ€”Officials swallow â€˜actual maliceâ€™ pill.â€

Bottom Line
State libel law cannot penalize criticism of public officials unless the speaker knew it was false or recklessly ignored the truth; mere inaccuracy, negligence, or presumed malice is constitutionally insufficient.

2. Shuttlesworth v. City of Birmingham, 394 U.S. 147 (1969)

â€” Warren Court strikes down discretionary parade-permit regime: citizens may ignore a â€œprior restraint without standards.â€

Facts & Procedural Posture

  • Good Friday, April 12, 1963: Civil-rights leader Rev. Fred Shuttlesworth led 52 Black demonstrators four blocks from a Birmingham church to protest segregation. Marchers stayed mostly on sidewalks; no traffic blocked.
  • They had no parade permit under Birmingham Code Â§ 1159, which required written application and allowed the City Commission to deny permits whenever â€œpublic welfare, peace, safety, health, decency, good order, morals or convenienceâ€ so â€œrequire.â€
  • Police arrested Shuttlesworth; he was convicted, sentenced to 90 days hard labor + fine. Alabama appellate courts affirmed. SCOTUS granted cert.

Petitionerâ€™s (Shuttlesworth) Theory

  1. Ordinance is an unconstitutional prior restraint: no narrow, objective standards; grants officials unbridled discretion.
  2. Where a licensing scheme is facially void, citizens may exercise First Amendment rights without first applying.
  3. Conviction violates Due Process because ordinance is overbroad and discriminatorily enforced against civil-rights protesters.

Respondentâ€™s (City) Theory

  1. Streets are a public resource; permits ensure orderly traffic and safety.
  2. Petitioner failed to exhaust administrative remediesâ€”could have applied earlier or pursued judicial review; cannot â€œself-license.â€
  3. Even if parts of ordinance vague, Court should construe it narrowly or uphold conviction for ==unpermitted use of streets.== ??

Issues Presented

  1. Does Â§ 1159, vesting permit decisions in officialsâ€™ subjective judgment, constitute an unconstitutional prior restraint?
  2. If a licensing law is unconstitutional on its face, may demonstrators be punished for marching without obtaining a permit?

Holding & Vote

  • 8 â€“ 0 (Stewart, J.): Ordinance vests â€œvirtually unbridled and absolute powerâ€â€”invalid under First & Fourteenth Amendments. Where a law is so void, citizens may ignore it and exercise speech rights with impunity; conviction reversed.

Core Reasoning (Stewart)

  1. Precedent forbids permit systems lacking â€œnarrow, objective, and definite standardsâ€ (e.g., Staub v. Baxley, Kunz v. New York). Words like â€œdecencyâ€ or â€œmoralsâ€ allow suppression of disfavored ideas.
  2. Prior restraint doctrine: When the right to speak hinges on an officialâ€™s unguided whim, the scheme is unconstitutional.
  3. Because Birminghamâ€™s ordinance was facially void, Shuttlesworth was entitled to disregard it; state may not imprison him for not begging permission to exercise a constitutional right.

Concurring Perspectives (Highlights)

  • Harlan, J.: Agrees ordinance unconstitutional but emphasizes practical absence of expedited review; without prompt procedure, permit denial effectively final, justifying civil-rights marchersâ€™ decision to proceed.
  • White, J. (separate): Stresses that narrow traffic-control rules are permissible; defect here is standardless discretion and discriminatory use.

Doctrinal Takeaways

  • Clarifies that void-for-vagueness + discretion in permit schemes equals facial invalidity.
  • Establishes citizensâ€™ right to â€œignoreâ€ such schemes (later cited in Cox v. Louisiana II, Papachristou).
  • Signals that permissible parade ordinances must: (a) contain objective criteria; (b) require prompt decision; (c) allow swift judicial review.

Impact & Legacy

  • Became cornerstone for modern time-, place-, and manner licensing jurisprudence; cities now draft detailed, content-neutral parade rules (attendance caps, insurance, fees) to survive Shuttlesworth scrutiny.
  • Frequently invoked in challenges to protest permitting after 9/11 and during Black Lives Matter demonstrations.

Socratic Traps & Answers

  • Q: City ordinance now denies permits for any march criticizing police during rush hourâ€”valid?
    A: Likely invalid: content-based and vested in official discretion, violating Shuttlesworth.
  • Q: If city had fixed standards (route capacity, safety) and offered 48-hour review, could it punish marchers without a permit?
    A: Yesâ€”constitutionally tailored scheme; protestors must comply or face civil penalties.
  • Q: Does Shuttlesworth forbid permit fees?
    A: Noâ€”reasonable, content-neutral fees tied to administrative costs are permissible (Forsyth County v. Nationalist Movement, 1992).

Mnemonic

â€œSHUTTLES can WORTHily launch without a license when officials steer by whim.â€

Bottom Line
A permit system that lets officials veto demonstrations for amorphous â€œpublic welfareâ€ reasons is a facial prior restraint; when such a law controls the streets, demonstrators may march without papersâ€”and the state cannot jail them for exercising their First Amendment freedoms.

3. New York Times Co. v. United States, 403 U.S. 713 (1971)

â€” â€œPentagon Papersâ€ decision: extraordinary prior restraints demand an extraordinary justification the government could not supply

Facts & Procedural Posture

  • Top-secret study leaked: In March 1971 RAND analyst Daniel Ellsberg photocopied a 7,000-page Defense Department history of U.S.-Vietnam policy (1945-1968).
  • June 13 â€“ 18: New York Times began serializing excerpts (â€œPentagon Papersâ€). The Nixon Administration sought an injunction under Â§ 793(e) & Â§Â§ 794â€“798 Espionage Act plus inherent executive power, alleging grave, immediate harm to national security.
  • S.D.N.Y. (Judge Gurfein): Issued a temporary restraining order; then, after in-camera review, denied a preliminary injunctionâ€”â€œThe security of the Nation is not at the ramparts alone.â€
  • 2d Cir. (per curiam): Reversed 5-3, remanding for detailed findings; continued the prior restraint pending appeal.
  • Meanwhile, Washington Post began publishing; D.D.C. denied injunction; D.C. Cir. affirmed 2-1.
  • With the press split, the Supreme Court granted cert & stay on June 25 and heard argument June 26, deciding June 30: the fastest merits disposition in Court history.

Petitionersâ€™ (U.S.) Theory

  1. ==Imminent, irreparable harm:== Release would compromise ongoing diplomatic initiatives (Paris peace talks), expose intelligence sources, and prolong the war.
  2. Courts must defer to executive assessments of national security; traditional criminal remedies are inadequate once secrets are public.
  3. Even if the First Amendment disfavors prior restraints generally, publication of ==classified information obtained unlawfully== is an exceptional case where equity may intervene.

Respondentsâ€™ (Times & Post) Theory

  1. Heavy presumption against prior restraints (Near v. Minnesota)â€”government bears a â€œheavy burdenâ€ of proof it has not met.
  2. The Papers are a historical study, not operational plans; governmentâ€™s assertions are conclusory and unsupported.
  3. Injunctions are tantamount to licensing; the proper remedy is after-the-fact prosecution, subject to Sullivanâ€Gertz standards.

Issues Presented

  1. Does the First Amendment permit the federal courts to enjoin newspapers from publishing classified, historically oriented documents absent a specific statute authorizing such restraint?
  2. What burden of proof must the United States satisfy to justify a prior restraint on publication?

Holding & Vote

  • Per curiam, 6 â€“ 3: Government failed to meet the â€œheavy burden of showing justification for the imposition of a prior restraint.â€ Injunctions vacated; publication may proceed.

Core Reasoning (Per curiam summary)

  • The First Amendment â€œleaves no room for governmental restraint of the pressâ€ unless the Government can show direct, immediate, and irreparable injury to the Nation or its peopleâ€”burden unmet here.

Concurring Opinions (Highlights)

  • Black (joined by Douglas): First Amendment is absoluteâ€”â€œno lawâ€ means no law; only a free press can expose official mistakes and deception.
  • Brennan: Prior restraint tolerable only if publication would â€œsurely result in direct, immediate, and irreparable damage to the Nationâ€; record contains no specific proof.
  • Stewart & White: Executive has broader constitutional responsibility for secrets, but courts may enjoin only when disclosure will â€œsurelyâ€ cause grave harm; Government offered mere surmise.
  • Marshall: Separation-of-powers barâ€”Congress has not authorized pre-publication injunctions; the courts cannot manufacture one.

Dissents (Highlights)

  • Harlan (joined by Burger & Blackmun): Criticizes â€œfreneticâ€†hasteâ€; argues for deference to the political branches and full fact-finding; would remand with injunction intact.
  • Blackmun: Potential lives at stake; presses for balancing national security versus freedom of the press, finds the scales uncertain.

Doctrinal Takeaways

  • Near-Sullivan lineage fortified: Prior restraints on speechâ€”even involving national securityâ€”carry an almost conclusive presumption of invalidity.
  • Burden on Government: must prove publication will lead to â€œdirect, immediate, and irreparable harmâ€; hypothetical or generalized harm is insufficient.
  • Leaves open the possibility of post-publication criminal liability, but erects sky-high barriers to pre-publication silence.

Impact & Legacy

  • Cemented the pressâ€™s watchdog role; accelerated investigative journalism on Watergate, CIA domestic spying, and later, digital-age leaks (Snowden, WikiLeaks).
  • Spawned continuing battles over classified-information leaks, yet no federal court has enjoined a mainstream outlet since.
  • Influences FOIA jurisprudence and debates over the Espionage Actâ€™s reach toward publishers.

Socratic Traps & Answers

  • Q: Could the Government obtain an injunction today if publication would reveal an imminent troop location?
    A: Possiblyâ€”the Times standard allows prior restraint if harm is specific, immediate, and catastrophic and proved with concrete evidence.
  • Q: Does Times immunize journalists from criminal prosecution under the Espionage Act?
    A: Noâ€”the Court expressly limited its holding to prior restraint; post-publication prosecutions remain theoretically available (though never tested against a newspaper).
  • Q: If a statute expressly barred publication of nuclear launch codes and provided for injunctions, would that satisfy Marshallâ€™s separation-of-powers concern?
    A: Statutory authority would remove one objection, but courts would still scrutinize whether the restraint meets the heavy-burden test for immediate, irreparable harm.

Mnemonic

â€œPENTAGON PAPERS: Press Prints, President Protestsâ€”Prior Restraint Falls.â€

Bottom Line
Absent an overwhelming, immediate threat proven with specificity, the Government cannot gag the press before publicationâ€”the First Amendmentâ€™s presumption against prior restraints reigns supreme, even when classified secrets are at stake.

Libel refers to written or visual defamationâ€”statements made in a fixed medium like articles, posters, cartoons, or digital images that injure someone's reputation. Slander refers to spoken defamationâ€”transient verbal statements that carry the same injurious effect but are traditionally treated with more evidentiary burden due to their ephemeral nature.

Prior restraint refers to a government action that prevents speech from being expressed before it occurs. This is constitutionally disfavored and subject to the strictest scrutiny under First Amendment doctrine. Unlike punishment after the fact, prior restraint seeks to stop the expression from ever reaching the public, which courts view as presumptively unconstitutional unless certain exacting standards are met.

The leading case on prior restraint is Southeastern Promotions Ltd. v. Conrad (1975). In this case, a theater group sought to present the controversial musical Hair in a municipal facility but was denied a permit. The Court ruled that this denial constituted an impermissible prior restraint because the permitting system lacked procedural safeguards and gave excessive discretion to officials. The decision clarified the constitutional requirements that any system of prior restraint must meet.

The Court laid out a three-part test for when prior restraint might be allowed:

  1. There must be an important or compelling governmental reason to impose the restraint (e.g., protecting national security in a narrowly defined context, avoiding imminent incitement to violence).
  2. The regulatory scheme must contain clear, narrowly drawn, and objective standards that leave little to no discretion in the hands of the licensing authority. Discretionary systems are prone to censorship based on content or viewpoint.
  3. There must be strong procedural safeguards, including:
    • A fair and timely hearing
    • A quick final decision
    • Availability of appellate review
      These ensure the speaker is not silenced indefinitely while the process drags on.

In this context, a first-come, first-served system may qualify as a clear standard because it minimizes discretion. If a permit is granted based solely on application timing and not on content or viewpoint, that suggests neutrality and fairness. However, the system must be consistently applied and not used to indirectly suppress certain viewpoints by, for instance, denying access to unpopular speakers through technicalities.

Judicial injunctions on speechâ€”court orders restraining parties from making certain statementsâ€”also implicate prior restraint principles. These are highly controversial because they amount to court-imposed censorship.

  • The collateral bar rule says that even if an injunction is later found unconstitutional, it must be obeyed until overturned through proper legal channels. Disobeying the order can lead to contempt sanctions regardless of its ultimate validity. This rule creates tension between obeying the law and protecting expressive freedom.
  • Judicial "gag orders" are a form of injunction usually applied in the context of ongoing trials (e.g., to prevent pretrial publicity from influencing a jury). These are subject to strict scrutiny and often invalidated unless they are absolutely necessary and narrowly tailored.

A declaratory injunction merely declares the rights and legal status of the partiesâ€”it does not mandate behavior. A full injunction, by contrast, is a coercive court order requiring or forbidding specific conduct, including expressive acts. Only the latter triggers prior restraint concerns.

Regarding national security, the Court has repeatedly emphasized that broad or vague references to â€œsecurityâ€ are insufficient. In New York Times Co. v. United States (1971) (the Pentagon Papers case), the governmentâ€™s attempt to enjoin publication on national security grounds failed because its justifications were too general and speculative. The Court held that security cannot be used as a blanket justification for suppressing speech; it must be specific, concrete, and imminent.

Overall, the doctrine of prior restraint is built on a distrust of government power to pre-approve speech. The constitutional preference is always for post hoc accountability (e.g., libel suits) rather than ex ante censorship.

Week 8 Office Hours Notes

  1. Carson v. Makin

  2. The question of whether Paid Days Off (PDOs) for menstrual cramping violate the Equal Protection Clause of the Fourteenth Amendment turns on whether the law classifies on the basis of sex and, if so, whether it satisfies intermediate scrutiny. Under Craig v. Boren and reinforced by United States v. Virginia (VMI), sex-based classifications must serve an important governmental interest and be substantially related to achieving that interest. The Court in VMI went further and demanded an â€œexceedingly persuasive justificationâ€ for such sex-based policies. A menstrual PDO policy would likely be viewed as facially sex-based, triggering intermediate scrutiny automatically. The state might argue it supports gender equity in the workforce, but it must demonstrate that the policy is not based on overbroad generalizations or stereotypes about womenâ€™s capacity for work.

    1. From a rational basis lens, this might pass easily, but the real question is whether it's sufficiently tailored. The tailoring inquiry focuses on over- and under-inclusiveness: is the policy grounded in objective medical thresholds (e.g., base level of pain), or does it substitute a moral or social value judgment for legal precision? For example, comparing flu-related medical leave (often permitted with documentation) versus menstrual pain (often stigmatized or minimized) may raise substantive equality concerns. If a policy gives 12 days of PDOs for medical reasons and includes menstrual pain as one eligible cause, thatâ€™s more neutralâ€”but a blanket carveout or extra leave solely for menstruation may face questions about scope, gender exclusivity, or economic impact (e.g., employer costs, tax implications). Relatedly, the tax status of menstrual products like tamponsâ€”often taxed as luxury items rather than exempt medical goodsâ€”raises issues under state constitutional equality clauses. States like California have cited state-level Equal Protection to prohibit taxing bottled water differently from other health-related items, hinting at analogous arguments for menstrual equity.
    2. This discussion connects to Carolene Products Footnote 4, which suggested that laws targeting â€œdiscrete and insular minoritiesâ€, especially when involving immutable characteristics (like sex or race), merit heightened scrutiny. This framework could arguably be extended to menstrual equity under gender-based immutability reasoning. A similar dynamic played out in public reaction to Chicagoâ€™s soda tax, where critics argued that the tax disproportionately impacted low-income communitiesâ€”highlighting how overbreadth and hidden class lines often permeate seemingly neutral health regulations. The logic would also map to abortion-related taxes or carveouts: for example, taxing abortion services while exempting other procedures may present an equal protection or undue burden claim, depending on the facts.

  3. Shifting to immigration and speech, the idea of deporting international students for anti-American speech (e.g., social media posts critical of U.S. values or DEI discourse) raises grave constitutional red flags. For noncitizens already physically present in the U.S., including those on F-1 visas tracked by SEVP (Student and Exchange Visitor Program), any such policy would likely amount to viewpoint discrimination, which is presumptively invalid under the First Amendment. As applied here, it would require the government to punish individuals based on the content of their political beliefs, triggering strict scrutiny and likely failing due to the absence of a compelling interest narrowly tailored to such speech. Additionally, due process protections would apply, especially since termination of SEVP status can result in immediate loss of legal presence and serious immigration consequences. While the federal government does enjoy broader power at the borderâ€”keeping people out via visa restrictionsâ€”it faces stricter constitutional limits when regulating people already inside the U.S. Any such measure would need to grapple with both procedural due process and substantive free speech doctrine.

  4. On the matter of foreign affairs and executive power, courts have long recognized that the judiciary lacks authority to interfere with foreign policy decisions of the Executive Branch. This deference is rooted in the political question doctrine, often invoked in cases like Zivotofsky v. Kerry or Haig v. Agee, where courts defer to the Presidentâ€™s unique competence in diplomacy and national security. However, this doesnâ€™t mean the executive power is unlimitedâ€”especially where individual constitutional rights are implicated domestically.

  5. This leads to the final question: if Canadaâ€™s public discourse or media turns hostile to Americans, can the U.S. government respond with travel bans, export controls, or digital censorship? Constitutionally, such retaliation raises serious First Amendment and commerce-related issues, particularly if these restrictions are applied domestically or affect U.S. citizens' access to foreign media, platforms, or markets. Even in the realm of foreign commerce, Congress and the Executive must act within enumerated powers (e.g., Commerce Clause, foreign affairs), and retaliatory actions cannot violate constitutional guaranteesâ€”including free speech, equal protection, or due processâ€”especially when impacting individuals inside U.S. borders. Blanket statements about â€œnational securityâ€ do not suffice; the government must provide specific, imminent, and concrete justifications for infringing core liberties. As in Pentagon Papers or Boumediene v. Bush, vague fears of propaganda or foreign hostility are not enough to suppress speech or impose sweeping restrictions. The Constitution does not permit ideological retaliation in the name of geopolitics without clear, lawful limits.

Week 9.1

1. Lemon v. Kurtzman & Earley v. DiCenso (companion), 403 U.S. 602 (1971)

â€” Burger Court crystallizes the modern Establishment-Clause framework: statutes aiding parochial schools must clear the three-part â€œLemon test.â€

Facts & Procedural Posture

  • Pennsylvania â€œPurchase of Servicesâ€ Act (1968): reimbursed non-public schoolsâ€”almost all Catholicâ€”for teachersâ€™ salaries, textbooks, and materials in secular subjects (math, modern languages, science, P.E.). Reimbursements flowed directly to the schools, subject to audits and accounting rules.
  • Rhode Island Salary Supplement Act (1969): paid 15 % salary bonuses directly to teachers of secular subjects in qualifying non-public elementary schoolsâ€”again, overwhelmingly Catholic. Applicants pledged not to teach religion and to use only materials approved for public schools.
  • Taxpayer plaintiffs (including Alton Lemon) alleged violation of Establishment & Free Exercise Clauses; three-judge district courts splitâ€”Pennsylvania law upheld, Rhode Island law struck. Appeals consolidated; Supreme Court noted first application of new Â§ 1253 review route.

Petitionersâ€™ (States) Theory

  1. Secular purpose: rescue financially imperiled private schools providing mandatory secular education, thus easing public-school overcrowding.
  2. Primary effect neutral: aid earmarked to non-religious instruction; explicit bans on religious content safeguard neutrality.
  3. No excessive entanglement: audits are routine fiscal oversight; comparable to health inspections or bus subsidies already upheld.

Respondentsâ€™ (Taxpayers) Theory

  1. Parochial schools are pervasively sectarian; money is inherently fungibleâ€”state funds inevitably advance religion.
  2. Continuous audits, salary monitoring, and per-pupil cost policing create intimate churchâ€“state supervisionâ€”an entanglement the Founders sought to avoid.
  3. Political divisiveness along religious lines is itself a forbidden entanglement.

Issues Presented

  1. Do statutes that subsidize secular instruction in church-related schools violate the Establishment Clause?
  2. What analytic framework governs such church-state aid questions?

Holding & Vote

  • 8 â€“ 0 on rule (Burger, C.J.), 8 â€“ 1 on application (White dissenting as to Pennsylvania): Both statutes are unconstitutional because they foster â€œexcessive government entanglement with religion.â€

Core Reasoning (Burger)

  1. Synthesizes prior precedents into a three-pronged test: a statute
    1. (i) must have a secular legislative purpose,
    2. (ii) its principal or primary effect must neither advance nor inhibit religion, and
    3. (iii) it must avoid excessive government entanglement with religion.
  2. Purpose here is secular, and effect arguably neutral, but continuous auditing of school accounts, policing teacher conduct, and monitoring per-pupil religious expenditures create pervasive, enduring oversightâ€”â€œa relationship pregnant with dangersâ€ of church dominance or state censorship.
  3. Cash subsidies risk political factionalism: fights over public funds will align with sectarian identity, precisely the evil the Clause anticipates.

Concurring Snapshots

  • Douglas: Once aid flows, fungibility guarantees advancement of faith; statutes fail all three prongs.
  • Brennan: Warns that even â€œsecularâ€ instruction in parochial schools inevitably transmits religious worldview.
  • White (partly dissenting): Would uphold Pennsylvania planâ€”sees no greater entanglement than textbook loans in Allen.

Doctrinal Takeaways

  • â€œLemon testâ€ becomes default metric for Establishment cases for decadesâ€”though later treated as â€œguidelines,â€ not talismanic.
  • Introduces entanglement as distinct from purpose/effect; recognizes political divisiveness as a constitutional harm.
  • Fuels wave of litigation striking or narrowing state-aid schemes until Agostini v. Felton (1997) relaxes prong â‘¡/â‘¢.

Impact & Legacy

  • Dooms most direct cash subsidies to parochial schools until voucher cases (Zelman, 2002) chart a new path via â€œprivate choice.â€
  • Supplies lower courts with a ready checklist; simultaneously criticized as overly rigid and indeterminate.
  • Term â€œexcessive entanglementâ€ enters constitutional lexicon, influencing faith-based-initiative debates.

Socratic Traps & Answers

  • Q: Would free state-provided Chromebooks to all students violate Lemon?
    A: Likely passâ€”neutral, secular hardware, minimal ongoing monitoring.
  • Q: Must states yank bus subsidies to parochial schools after Lemon?
    A: Noâ€”Everson already upheld transportation; effect neutral, oversight trivial.
  • Q: If a statute required quarterly catechism audits, what prong fails first?
    A: Entanglement (â…¢) via invasive surveillance.

Mnemonic

â€œLemon Squeeze: Purposeâ€“Effectâ€“Entanglement.â€

Bottom Line
State aid to religious schools survives only if it has a secular aim, a neutral effect, and avoids deep churchâ€“state entanglement; Pennsylvania and Rhode Island flunk on the last, birthing the durable but controversial Lemon framework.

2. Lee v. Weisman, 505 U.S. 577 (1992)

â€” Kennedy for the Rehnquist Court extends school-prayer limits: graduation invocations violate the Establishment Clause under a new â€œsubtle coercionâ€ test.

Facts & Procedural Posture

  • Providence, RI public schools: long-standing policy lets principals invite clergy to deliver non-sectarian invocation & benediction at middle- and high-school graduations; clergy receive NCCJ â€œGuidelines for Civic Occasions.â€
  • 1989: Principal Robert Lee asked Rabbi Leslie Gutterman to pray at Nathan Bishop Middle School ceremony; provided draft guidance but did not script text.
  • Parent Daniel Weisman sought TRO; denied. After ceremony, family sued to enjoin future prayers. District court & 1st Circuit ruled for Weisman; Supreme Court granted cert.

Petitionersâ€™ (School District & U.S.) Theory

  1. Prayers are ceremonial deism echoing historic traditions (Thanksgiving, inaugural invocations); minimal, nonsectarian, voluntary attendance.
  2. Any discomfort is de minimis; students may sit or skip graduation.
  3. If Lemon applies, the policy has secular purposes (solemnity), neutral effects, and negligible entanglement.

Respondentâ€™s (Weisman) Theory

  1. Graduation is effectively obligatory; state-directed prayer coerces students to participate or appear disrespectfulâ€”violating principle of governmental neutrality in matters of conscience.
  2. Pre-service vetting of clergy and insistence on nonsectarian content shows state control of religion, forbidden whether sectarian or not.
  3. Lemon and school-prayer precedents (Engel, Schempp) forbid religious exercises in the public-school context.

Issues Presented

  1. Does inclusion of clergy-led, nonsectarian prayers in public-school graduations contravene the Establishment Clause?
  2. Is psychological or peer pressure a constitutionally cognizable form of coercion?

Holding & Vote

  • 5 â€“ 4 (Kennedy, J.): State-sponsored graduation prayers are unconstitutional; government may not coerce participation in religious exercise through social pressure in the school setting.

Core Reasoning (Kennedy)

  1. Coercion principle: Even subtle pressure on impressionable students to stand or remain reverent is â€œas real as any overt compulsion.â€
  2. State direction: Officials selected clergy, set prayer parameters, and placed invocation within official programâ€”amounting to state endorsement.
  3. ==Opt-out not adequate;== missing graduation sacrifices a once-in-a-lifetime rite. The Free Exercise â€œaccommodationâ€ rationale cannot override core prohibition.
  4. Decision reached without overruling Lemon but treats entanglement and endorsement through lens of coercion.

Concurring / Dissenting Highlights

  • Blackmun (conc.): Embraces Lemon; prayer fails all three prongs.
  • Souter (conc.): ==Historical practices== do not justify graduation prayer; rejects â€œcivic religion.â€
  • Scalia (dissent, joined by Rehnquist, White, Thomas): Majority invents â€œpsychological coercionâ€ unheard of in 1791; tradition and neutrality permit nonsectarian civic prayer.

Doctrinal Takeaways

  • Introduces â€œpsychological coercionâ€ testâ€”particularly potent in primary/secondary schools. -- contrast with lemon: replacing the coercion test with the
  • Marks shift from Lemon toward endorsement/coercion hybrid later echoed in Santa Fe ISD v. Doe (2000).
  • Clarifies that nonsectarian prayer is not a constitutional safe harbor when government orchestrates the event.

Impact & Legacy

  • Graduation ceremonies nationwide drop official clergy prayers, replace with â€œmoments of silenceâ€ or student remarks.
  • Coercion analysis resurfaces in public-monument and pledge-of-allegiance litigation.
  • Sparks intense debate over civic traditions versus strict separationâ€”dialogue amplified by Justice Scaliaâ€™s polemical dissent.

Socratic Traps & Answers

  • Q: Would a student-initiated prayer, chosen by lottery, pass Lee?
    A: Probably notâ€”Santa Fe held even â€œstudent-ledâ€ prayers at football games unconstitutional when school-sponsored.
  • Q: Legislative chaplains (Marsh v. Chambers) still OK?
    A: Yesâ€”Court distinguishes adult legislatures from compulsory school settings.
  • Q: Does Lee doom â€œunder Godâ€ in the Pledge?
    A: Not necessarily; Pledge recitation is opt-out and historically embeddedâ€”though Scalia flagged the tension.

Mnemonic

â€œLEE: Leave Ecclesiastics at the Exit.â€

Bottom Line
When public-school officials orchestrate even â€œnonsectarianâ€ prayer at a graduation, the subtle but real pressure on students constitutes unconstitutional state-coerced religious exerciseâ€”cementing the idea that school events must remain prayer-free to honor liberty of conscience.

3. Van Orden v. Perry, 545 U.S. 677 (2005)

â€” Rehnquist Court splits over a granite Ten Commandments on Texas Capitol grounds: historical context saves what â€œLemonâ€ alone might sour.

Facts & Procedural Posture

  • Austin, TX (1961): The Fraternal Order of Eagles donated a 6-foot granite monument inscribed with the Ten Commandments; the state installed it on a walkway leading to the Capitol. The 22-acre grounds already featured 17+ other historical and patriotic displays (heroes of Texas Revolution, Bill of Rights tribute, Korean-War memorial, etc.).
  • Thomas Van Orden, an Austin attorney who often passed the monument, filed suit in 2001 under 42 U.S.C. Â§ 1983, alleging violation of the Establishment Clause.
  • U.S. District Court (W.D. Tex.) granted summary judgment for Texas; Fifth Circuit affirmed. The Supreme Court noted probable jurisdiction and heard argument the same day as McCreary County v. ACLU (courthouse Commandmentsâ€”later struck).

Petitionerâ€™s (Van Orden) Theory

  1. Religious endorsement: A stand-alone Decalogue on government property sends the message that the state endorses Judeo-Christian theology.
  2. Fails Lemon test: Neither secular purpose nor primary secular effect; presence of other monuments irrelevant because none share religious content.
  3. No genuine historical context: Monument was installed as part of an Eagles campaign to promote religion and morality.

Respondentâ€™s (Texas) Theory

  1. Dual secular and moral significance: Ten Commandments influenced Western law; monument acknowledges that heritage among many others.
  2. Passive display: Silent, permanent, non-proselytizing; no schoolchildren or captive audience coerced.
  3. Historical practice: Governments long reference the Decalogue (e.g., SCOTUS frieze); public understands it chiefly as a legal-historic symbol.

Issues Presented
Does the presence of a Ten Commandments monument on state-capitol grounds violate the Establishment Clause of the First Amendment, as applied to the states through the Fourteenth Amendment?

Holding & Vote

  • 5 â€“ 4 to uphold (Rehnquist, C.J., plurality; Breyer concurrence supplies the fifth vote): The monument is constitutional given its passive, historical context and the nationâ€™s tradition of acknowledging religionâ€™s role in public life.

Opinion Breakdown

Alignment Key Points
Plurality (Rehnquist, joined by Scalia, Kennedy, Thomas) â€¢ Lemon is not a useful compass here; Establishment jurisprudence must be shaped by â€œthe relief sought and the context.â€ â€¢ Decalogue has dual significanceâ€”moral & historical; its 40-year presence among many secular monuments shows a â€œrespectful accommodationâ€, not endorsement.
Concurrence (Breyer) â€” controlling vote â€¢ Rejects formal tests; decides by â€œlegal judgmentâ€ balancing text, history, purpose, and physical setting. â€¢ Monumentâ€™s long, controversy-free existence & mixed secular surroundings create no real risk of divisive religious government. â€¢ Emphasizes â€œborderlineâ€ nature: slight changes (new display, school yard) could flip result.
Dissent (Stevens, joined by Ginsburg) â€¢ Purpose & effect plainly religious; context cannot convert a scriptural proclamation into secular history.
Dissent (Oâ€™Connor) â€¢ Warns that government endorsement alienates non-adherents; would apply Lemon/Endorsement.
Dissent (Souter, joined by Stevens & Ginsburg) â€¢ Historical influence does not excuse overtly sacred text; absence of challenge for 40 years proves only inertia.

Doctrinal Takeaways

  1. Context matters. Passive, long-standing displays with mixed surroundings can survive even if overtly religious.
  2. Lemon not abandoned but sidelined. Plurality treats it as one tool, not master test; Breyer applies â€œpragmatic judgment.â€
  3. Decided the same day as McCreary, producing twin precedents: new courthouse Commandments struck (religious purpose), old capitol Commandments upheld (historical context).
  4. Sets stage for â€œhistorical practices & understandingsâ€ approach later emphasized in Town of Greece v. Galloway (2014) and foreshadows the Courtâ€™s post-2019 drift away from Lemon.

Impact & Legacy

  • States & cities often cite Van Orden to defend long-standing religious monuments (e.g., veteransâ€™ memorials).
  • Lower courts draw a durational/contextual distinction: aged monuments â†’ likely constitutional; newly erected religious displays â†’ greater scrutiny.
  • Fuels scholarly debate over whether Establishment analysis should hinge on history & coercion rather than Lemon/endorsement.

Socratic Traps & Answers

  • Q: Could Texas now add a new Ten Commandments statue beside the old?
    A: Riskyâ€”contemporaneous government purpose would be examined; absence of historical pedigree may doom it (McCreary rationale).
  • Q: What if the monument stood alone on the grounds?
    A: Solitary sacred text with no secular companions weakens â€œhistorical tableauâ€ defense; likely unconstitutional.
  • Q: Does Van Orden overrule Lemon?
    A: No; plurality merely declines to apply it. Later cases chip away but stop short of formal overruling until Kennedy v. Bremerton Sch. Dist. (2022) effectively retires it.

Mnemonic

â€œVan Orden â†’ Context orders the Commandments.â€

Bottom Line
A passive Ten Commandments monument that has quietly stood for decades among numerous secular tributes on a state-capitol lawn does not amount to governmental endorsement of religionâ€”its historical context and absence of coercion tip the constitutional balance, illustrating the Courtâ€™s shift from rigid formulas to pragmatic, context-sensitive judgment.

In the realm of First Amendment religion cases, the Supreme Court has long struggled to draw a stable line between establishment and free exercise, oscillating between the ideals of strict separation, neutrality, and accommodation. The most famous doctrinal test arose in Lemon v. Kurtzman (1971), where the Court articulated a three-pronged framework to evaluate whether government action violates the Establishment Clause: (1) the law must have a secular legislative purpose, (2) its primary effect must neither advance nor inhibit religion, and (3) it must not foster excessive government entanglement with religion. Although never formally overturned, the Lemon test has been hollowed out and increasingly bypassed by the Court in favor of neutrality or historical practice tests.

In Carson v. Makin (2022), the Court effectively rejected Lemon without saying so outright, holding that Maineâ€™s exclusion of religious schools from a tuition-assistance program violated the Free Exercise Clause. The ruling embraced a neutrality-based approach, where government cannot exclude religious entities from a public benefit solely due to their religious status. The shift away from Lemon reflects what scholars like Philip Kurland advocated decades ago: formal neutrality, where the state neither aids nor targets religion per se, but must remain equidistant from all beliefs. This approach gained traction in Van Orden v. Perry (2005), where a Ten Commandments monument on state capitol grounds was upheld due to its passive historical significance, signaling that context and history matter more than rigid doctrinal tests.

By contrast, the accommodation/equality approachâ€”seen in cases like Lee v. Weisman (1992)â€”accepts some government interaction with religion so long as it does not coerce participation or endorse religious orthodoxy. In Lee, the Court invalidated clergy-led prayers at public school graduations, emphasizing the psychological pressure on students and reaffirming that the state must not organize religious exercises in public education. This position reflects the liberty-centered theory of the Establishment Clause: not that religion is dangerous, but that state power corrupts voluntary conscience when it aligns itself with belief.

Where the government discriminates among religions, or favors one sect over another, the Court applies strict scrutinyâ€”even if it doesnâ€™t always name it as such. Any law that explicitly prefers Christianity, Judaism, or Islam over others is presumptively invalid unless it satisfies a compelling interest, which is almost never possible. Importantly, even when the Court applies the Lemon test, the first two prongs functionally mirror strict scrutiny: if a law lacks a secular purpose or its primary effect advances religion, then no amount of â€œimportant government interestâ€ can salvage itâ€”a hallmark of strict scrutiny logic cloaked in formalism.

Prayer in public institutions remains a key battleground. In Engel v. Vitale (1962), the Court struck down the recitation of a state-composed prayer in public schools, declaring it unconstitutional even though students could opt out. The problem wasnâ€™t coercionâ€”it was the symbolic union of church and state. Wallace v. Jaffree (1985) extended this to invalidate a "moment of silence" in public schools where the legislative history showed it was intended to reintroduce prayer. Yet in Marsh v. Chambers (1983), the Court upheld official legislative prayers at the start of sessions, noting that such practices were deeply rooted in historical tradition. The key takeaway is that schools and students are uniquely vulnerable, while legislatures composed of adults and elected officials are given more leeway under the historical-practice exception.

The symbolic use of religious imagery, such as the Christian cross, continues to provoke disagreement. In public settings, a cross may be challenged as an endorsement of religion, but the context matters. For example, a cross-shaped tombstone marking a soldierâ€™s grave may be seen as a personal religious symbol rather than state endorsement. Justice Scalia, in oral arguments and dissents, often criticized excessive sensitivity to such displays, suggesting that not all religious symbols are coercive or unconstitutional. This theme reappears in Carson v. Makin, where the stateâ€™s exclusion of religious use (not just religious status) was struck down, further shifting the balance toward religious equality in public funding.

Finally, in broader free speech contexts with national security implications, your notes reference New York Times Co. v. United States (1971)â€”the Pentagon Papers case. While the per curiam opinion blocked the government's attempt to impose prior restraint on publication, the concurrence by Justice Stewart (joined by Justice White) warned of the real risks of secrecy and emphasized the pressâ€™s structural role in checking executive abuse. Stewart wrote that â€œthe only effective restraint upon executive policy and power...may lie in an enlightened citizenry,â€ emphasizing that vague invocations of â€œsecurityâ€ cannot justify censorship. This ties back to religious liberty and free speech: the government cannot rely on broad generalities or unverified fears to justify the suppression or imposition of belief.

In sum, the Courtâ€™s religion doctrine today is a fragmented mosaic: strict scrutiny for religious discrimination, weakened Lemon for establishment cases, a growing tilt toward neutrality and accommodation, and an undercurrent of concern about coercion, symbolism, and public context.

Week 9.2

1. Sherbert v. Verner, 374 U.S. 398 (1963)

â€” Brennan, writing for a 7 â€“ 2 Warren Court, inaugurates the modern â€œcompelling-interest/least-restrictive-meansâ€ test for Free Exercise: South Carolina may not deny unemployment benefits to a Seventh-day Adventist who refuses Saturday work.

Facts & Procedural Posture

  • South Carolina textile worker Adell Sherbert (Seventh-day Adventist) was fired for declining Saturday shifts and repeatedly turned down jobs requiring Saturday labor.
  • State Unemployment Commission found her â€œunavailable for workâ€ and denied benefits under statutory language disqualifying claimants who â€œfail, without good cause, to accept suitable work.â€
  • State trial court and South Carolina Supreme Court affirmed, holding the rule imposed no unconstitutional burden. Sherbert appealed.

Appellantâ€™s Theory (Sherbert)

  1. Denial of benefits pressures her to choose between faith and livelihoodâ€”an unconstitutional penalty on religious exercise.
  2. Conditioning a public benefit on abandoning Sabbath observance violates Free Exercise, regardless of indirect form.
  3. Stateâ€™s fear of fraudulent claims is speculative and can be policed by less-restrictive alternatives.

Stateâ€™s Theory

  1. Rule neutrally requires all claimants be â€œavailableâ€; it does not target religion.
  2. Administering individual exemptions would jeopardize the fund and burden employers with scheduling uncertainty.
  3. Benefits are a statutory â€œprivilege,â€ not a right. (Rejected by Court.)

Issues

  1. Does disqualifying a Sabbatarian for refusing Saturday work substantially burden Free Exercise?
  2. If so, does the State have a compelling interest justifying that burden?

Holding & Vote

  • 7 â€“ 2: Judgment reversed.
  • Free Exercise is burdened; no compelling state interest supports denial.

Core Reasoning (Brennan)

  1. Substantial Burden: Forcing Sherbert to choose between faith and benefits is â€œas real as a fine.â€
  2. Strict Scrutiny: Only the â€œgravest abuses, endangering paramount interestsâ€ permit such infringement. Potential malingering is unsupported in record and could be addressed by narrower regulation.
  3. Neutrality vs. Establishment: Granting benefits is an accommodation, not establishment; it simply places Sabbatarians on equal footing.
  4. Rejects notion that public benefits may be denied as mere â€œprivileges.â€

Concurrences / Dissents

  • Stewart (conc.): Would invalidate on both Free Exercise and Equal Protection grounds.
  • Harlan (joined by White) (diss.): Sees no discrimination; State need not create religion-based exemptions and warns Court is singling out religion for special treatment.

Doctrinal Takeaways

  • Birth of the Sherbert test (substantial burden â†’ compelling interest + least-restrictive means).
  • Signals robust protection for religious exemptions in benefit contexts; later applied in Thomas, Hobbie.
  • Foreshadows distinction between accommodation and establishment.

Impact & Legacy

  • Became lynchpin of Free Exercise analysis until narrowed by Smith (1990).
  • Prompted many states to craft explicit Sabbath exceptions; influenced Title VII religious-accommodation doctrine.

Socratic Traps & Answers

  • Q: Would the result differ if Sherbert sought exemption from a criminal law (e.g., truancy)?
    A: Likely yes under Smith; criminal prohibitions receive more deference.
  • Q: Must States always honor religious work-schedule claims?
    A: Noâ€”may deny if they prove a specific, compelling need unmet by narrower means.

Mnemonic

â€œSherbert Fridayâ€”Saturday work denied, State must justify.â€

Bottom Line
Denying benefits because Sabbath observance limits job availability is an unconstitutional burden unless the State can pass strict scrutiny; the decision ushers in the Sherbert balancing era.

2. Wisconsin v. Yoder, 406 U.S. 205 (1972)

â€” Burger for a 7 â€“ 0 (Douglas conc./diss. in part) Court: Compulsory high-school attendance beyond 8th grade violates Amish parentsâ€™ Free Exercise.

Facts & Procedural Posture

  • Old Order Amish & Conservative Mennonite parents (Yoder, Miller, Yutzy) convicted and fined $5 each for refusing to send children (14-15 yrs) to school past 8th grade, contrary to Wisconsinâ€™s compulsory-attendance law.
  • State courts upheld convictions; Wisconsin Supreme Court affirmed. U.S. Supreme Court noted probable jurisdiction.

Parentsâ€™ Theory

  1. High-school environment contradicts core Amish tenets of separation, humility, agrarian life; threatens childrenâ€™s salvation.
  2. Pierce recognizes parental liberty to direct childrenâ€™s upbringing; compulsory schooling gravely burdens religion.
  3. Amish provide robust vocational training (on-farm apprenticeships), satisfying civic preparedness.

Stateâ€™s Theory

  1. Universal education to 16 is essential for citizenship and self-reliance.
  2. Exemptions risk leaving children unprepared if they leave community.
  3. Statute is facially neutral and uniformly applied; no targeted discrimination.

Issues

  1. Does forcing Amish children to attend school to age 16 violate parentsâ€™ Free Exercise rights?
  2. Is the Stateâ€™s interest sufficiently compelling to override those rights?

Holding & Vote

  • Affirmed 7 â€“ 0. Compulsory attendance law, as applied, violates Free Exercise; State interests not compelling here.

Core Reasoning (Chief Justice Burger)

  1. Substantial Burden: Law coerces conduct â€œundeniably at oddsâ€ with 300-year-old religious practice, threatening survival of Amish culture.
  2. Compelling-Interest Test Applied:
    • Civic preparation interest is legitimate but minimal incremental benefit past 8th grade given effective Amish vocational training.
    • No evidence exempted children become burdens on society.
  3. Narrow Tailoring: State failed to explore options like testing or part-time schooling.
  4. Distinguishes secular lifestyle choices (e.g., Thoreau) from deeply rooted religious obligation.

Douglas Separate Opinion

  • Agrees as to respondents whose children voiced no objection, but would remand to consider individual childâ€™s wishes, emphasizing childrenâ€™s own religious liberty.

Doctrinal Takeaways

  • Expands Sherbert strict-scrutiny regime to parental-rights hybrid cases.
  • Recognizes communal religious life as part of Free Exercise.
  • Introduces notion that incremental educational benefit must be weighed against religious harm.

Impact & Legacy

  • Provides model for modern home-schooling statutes and religious-school exemptions.
  • Cited as â€œhybrid rightsâ€ precedent in Smith.
  • Invoked in debates over vaccine mandates, child labor in religious communities.

Socratic Traps & Answers

  • Q: Would a similar claim by a secular communal group succeed?
    A: Unlikely; Court grounded protection in religious obligation, not mere philosophy.
  • Q: Could the State require standardized competency exams instead?
    A: Probably yesâ€”less restrictive, addresses civic literacy without daily attendance.

Mnemonic

â€œYoder Younger Than 16â€”Amish stay home after eighth.â€

Bottom Line
When a compulsory-education law threatens to destroy a coherent religious community, and the State cannot show a specific, necessary benefit unobtainable by narrower means, the Free Exercise Clause prevails.

3. Employment Division v. Smith, 494 U.S. 872 (1990)

â€” Scalia for a 6 â€“ 3 Rehnquist Court narrows Free Exercise: Neutral, generally applicable criminal laws need not grant religious exemptions (peyote use), overruling Sherbert balancing outside unemployment-benefit context.

Facts & Procedural Posture

  • Alfred Smith & Galen Black, counselors at private rehab clinic, were fired for ingesting peyote during Native American Church sacrament.
  • Oregon denied unemployment benefits, labeling peyote use â€œwork-related misconduct.â€
  • Oregon Supreme Court held denial unconstitutional and, on remand, that Oregonâ€™s peyote ban violated Free Exercise. U.S. Supreme Court granted cert.

Claimantsâ€™ Theory

  1. Denial of benefits burdens religious practice; under Sherbert test State must show compelling interest.
  2. Oregon historically never prosecuted religious peyote use; exemption would not undermine drug policy.

Stateâ€™s Theory

  1. Uniform enforcement of Schedule I drug prohibition is a neutral, generally applicable criminal law.
  2. Compelling-interest test should not override democratically enacted laws of general applicability.

Issues

  1. Does the Free Exercise Clause require a religious exemption from a neutral, generally applicable criminal drug law?
  2. Must Oregon nonetheless pay unemployment benefits to those fired for such religiously motivated conduct?

Holding & Vote

  • Reversed 6 â€“ 3. Free Exercise does not bar enforcement of neutral, generally applicable laws; Oregon may deny benefits.

Core Reasoning (Scalia)

  1. Historical Rule: The Constitution protects belief, not actions violating neutral laws (citing Reynolds polygamy).
  2. Neutral/Generally Applicable Standard: Only laws aimed at religion trigger strict scrutiny; otherwise, rational basis suffices.
  3. Sherbert Limited: Compelling-interest test confined to unemployment-benefit cases or â€œhybridâ€ rights (religion + speech/parenting).
  4. Warning that universal exemptions would make every citizen â€œa law unto himself,â€ risking anarchy.

Concurrence (Oâ€™Connor)

  • Agrees with result but insists Sherbert should remain governing; finds Oregonâ€™s interest compelling here.

Dissent (Blackmun, joined by Brennan & Marshall)

  • Accuses majority of abandoning precedent; argues State failed to prove compelling interest or show peyoteâ€™s religious use harms public health.

Doctrinal Takeaways

  • Establishes Smith rule: Neutral, generally applicable laws need no religious exemptions.
  • Sparks bipartisan backlash â†’ Religious Freedom Restoration Act (RFRA) 1993, reinstating Sherbert test federally; many state RFRAs follow.
  • Introduces â€œhybrid rightsâ€ dicta, seldom applied with force.
  • Leaves open strict scrutiny for targeted laws (Lukumi, 1993).

Impact & Legacy

  • Courts now first ask whether a law is neutral & generally applicable; only then consider compelling-interest test.
  • RFRA litigation (e.g., Hobby Lobby, 2014) occupies center stage where Smith does not control (federal action).
  • Ongoing debate on scope of neutrality in cases like Fulton v. Philadelphia (2021).

Socratic Traps & Answers

  • Q: Would Smith win under federal RFRA today?
    A: Likely yesâ€”RFRA restores strict scrutiny for federal (not state) law; but Oregon repealed peyote ban for religious use after the case.
  • Q: Are vaccine mandates â€œneutral and generally applicableâ€?
    A: Depends on breadth of secular exemptions; differential treatment triggers Lukumi strict scrutiny.

Mnemonic

â€œSmith Slices Sherbertâ€”No exemptions from neutral laws.â€

Bottom Line
Employment Division v. Smith reorients Free Exercise analysis: unless a statute targets religion or implicates another constitutional right, the government need not justify denying exemptionsâ€”leaving accommodation to legislatures and prompting RFRAâ€™s enactment.

4. Church of the Lukumi Babalu Aye, Inc. v. City of Hialeah, 508 U.S. 520 (1993)

â€” Kennedy for a fractured but unanimous Court resurrects strict scrutiny where a facially neutral ordinance targets religion; Hialeahâ€™s anti-â€œsacrificeâ€ laws flunk neutrality, general applicability, and narrow tailoring.

Facts & Procedural Posture

  • SanterÃ­a church (Lukumi) buys property in Hialeah, FL, announces plans for worship entailing ritual animal sacrifice (later cooked and eaten).
  • City Council emergency session: passes four ordinances prohibiting â€œunnecessary killing of animalsâ€ and possession for â€œsacrifice,â€ exempting kosher slaughter, fishing, hunting, medical research, pest control.
  • District court upholds ordinances; Eleventh Circuit affirms. Supreme Court grants cert.

Petitionersâ€™ (Church) Theory

  1. Ordinances surgically strike SanterÃ­a practiceâ€”impermissible religious targeting.
  2. Even if facially neutral, selective exemptions demolish general applicability; strict scrutiny applies.
  3. City cannot show compelling interest narrowly servedâ€”public health claims speculative; inspection regimes already exist.

Respondentâ€™s (Hialeah) Theory

  1. Laws are neutral public-health measures mirroring federal Humane Slaughter Act.
  2. No discrimination: prohibitions apply to all â€œritualâ€ killing.
  3. Stateâ€™s interests in animal welfare and waste disposal are compelling.

Issues Presented

  1. When does a facially neutral ordinance become unconstitutional by targeting religion?
  2. Do selective secular exemptions destroy general applicability, triggering strict scrutiny?

Holding & Vote

  • 9 â€“ 0 judgment (Kennedy, J.): Ordinances violate Free Exercise; not neutral or generally applicable. (Scalia, Souter, Blackmun, Oâ€™Connor each concur separately.)

Core Reasoning (Kennedy)

  1. Neutrality inquiry: Look beyond text to object, design, and operation; legislative minutes reveal hostility to SanterÃ­a.
  2. General applicability: Multiple secular exemptions render ban underinclusive, undermining asserted interests.
  3. Triggered strict scrutiny â†’ Cityâ€™s interests (public health, animal cruelty) under-inclusive and achievable through less-restrictive means (licensing, inspections).
  4. Confirms Smith permits exemptions for laws not neutral/generally applicable.

Concurrences

  • Scalia: Neutrality judged by text, not subjective motive; joins except dicta on motive.
  • Souter: Questions Smith; would revive Sherbert in all free-exercise cases.
  • Blackmun/Oâ€™Connor: Agree hostility but also find compelling-interest failure.

Doctrinal Takeaways

  • Targeting + underinclusion = fatal.
  • Establishes two-step pre-threshold: neutrality and general applicability.
  • Shows Smith is not a free pass for state hostility; strict scrutiny remains potent.

Impact & Legacy

  • Blueprint for later exemptions litigation (Fulton v. Philadelphia, 2021).
  • Invoked by lower courts striking COVID-19 worship limits that singled out churches (Tandon, 2021).
  • Clarifies that secular exemptions erode state claims of necessity.

Socratic Traps & Answers

  • Q: Would a total ban on all animal slaughter except emergencies survive?
    A: Possiblyâ€”neutral & generally applicable â†’ Smith rational-basis, but may fail under Due Process/property claims.
  • Q: Does Lukumi protect sacramental drug use?
    A: Only if a law targets the practice; neutral narcotics laws still governed by Smith unless RFRA applies federally.

Mnemonic

â€œLukumi Lops Lopsided Laws.â€

Bottom Line
Even after Smith, government may not single out a faith through underinclusive, exemption-ridden statutes; such targeting triggers strict scrutiny, which Hialeahâ€™s ordinances could not survive.

5. Kennedy v. Bremerton School District, 597 U.S. ___ (2022)

â€” Gorsuch for the Roberts Court buries Lemon, holds a public-school football coachâ€™s midfield post-game prayer is protected Free Exercise & Free Speech, rejecting â€œendorsementâ€ fears absent actual coercion.

Facts & Procedural Posture

  • Joseph Kennedy, assistant football coach at Bremerton (WA) High, developed practice (2008) of brief, silent 50-yard-line prayer after games; players sometimes voluntarily joined.
  • 2015: District orders cessation to avoid Establishment Clause issues; Kennedy continues, is put on leave, contract not renewed.
  • Kennedy sues under Free Exercise & Free Speech (42 U.S.C. Â§ 1983).
  • District court & Ninth Circuit grant/affirm summary judgment for school. Supreme Court grants cert.

Petitionerâ€™s (Kennedy) Theory

  1. Prayer was personal, private speech outside official dutiesâ€”protected expression.
  2. Districtâ€™s actions were viewpoint discrimination and burdened Free Exercise; failure to renew contract penalizes religious observance.
  3. No Establishment problem: activity happened after official play, voluntary, no student coercion.

Respondentâ€™s (School District) Theory

  1. Coach was â€œon dutyâ€â€”speech attributable to the State under Garcetti; district may control to avoid Establishment Clause violation.
  2. Students feel pressure to conform to coachâ€™s display; Lee/coercion concerns.
  3. Lemon/endorsement test requires avoiding reasonable perception of official religious endorsement.

Issues Presented

  1. Is a public-school employeeâ€™s brief, post-game prayer private speech protected by Free Speech & Free Exercise?
  2. Does allowing such prayer violate the Establishment Clause, justifying school prohibition?

Holding & Vote

  • 6 â€“ 3 (Gorsuch, J.): Bremerton violated Kennedyâ€™s constitutional rights; no Establishment Clause bar.

Core Reasoning (Gorsuch)

  1. Speech classification: Post-game, no coaching duties; prayer therefore private speech â†’ not government speech.
  2. Free Exercise burden: District conditioned employment on forgoing religious exercise; triggers strict scrutiny, unmet by speculative endorsement fears.
  3. Establishment Clause analysis: Rejects Lemon / endorsement as having â€œlong ago been abrogatedâ€; adopts â€œhistory & traditionâ€ approachâ€”brief personal prayer never historically banned.
  4. Coercion test: No evidence students were compelled; mere observation â‰  coercion.
  5. Harmony principle: Free Exercise & Free Speech rights complement, not conflict with, Establishment Clause.

Concurrences / Dissents

  • Thomas (conc.): Questions Garcetti scope; government-employee speech doctrine needs narrowing.
  • Alito (conc.): Emphasizes limits of government-speech label.
  • Sotomayor (dissent joined by Breyer, Kagan): Majority ignores factual record of student pressure; warns decision weakens church-state separation and public-school neutrality.

Doctrinal Takeaways

  • Formal retirement of Lemon; Establishment analysis now driven by historical practices + coercion.
  • Strengthens Free Exercise in public-employee context, narrowing government-speech doctrine.
  • Underscores coercionâ€”not mere offenseâ€”as touchstone for school-prayer cases.

Impact & Legacy

  • Signals Courtâ€™s broader movement toward history-and-tradition tests (see Dobbs, Bruen).
  • Invigorates challenges to â€œno-religionâ€ policies in public employment and public square.
  • School districts revise policies to focus on actual coercion rather than perceived endorsement.

Socratic Traps & Answers

  • Q: Could coach pray before kickoff as players stretch?
    A: Riskierâ€”still within duties; district may argue government speech. Outcome depends on factual â€œon-dutyâ€ analysis.
  • Q: May a district now sponsor a student-led prayer over loudspeaker?
    A: Unlikelyâ€”compulsory-audience + school sponsorship revives Santa Fe coercion problem.
  • Q: Does Kennedy overrule Lee v. Weisman?
    A: Not expressly; but limits Lee to situations of actual, not perceived, coercion.

Mnemonic

â€œKennedyâ€™s Kneeâ€”Prayer OK if free.â€

Bottom Line
The Court cements a speech-plus-religious-exercise right for public employees when they are not actively performing duties, discards Lemon, and re-anchors Establishment jurisprudence in historical practice and absence of real coercionâ€”expanding space for individual religious expression in public schools.

Week 10.1

1. District of Columbia v. Heller, 554 U.S. 570 (2008)

â€” Scalia for a 5 â€“ 4 Roberts Court holds the Second Amendment protects an individual right to keep a handgun in the home for self-defense, striking D.C.â€™s total handgun ban and trigger-lock rule.

Facts & Procedural Posture

  • D.C. law: flat ban on civilian handguns; long-guns in the home had to be disassembled/locked.
  • Dick Heller, a special police officer, was denied a permit to keep a functional handgun at home and sued under the Second Amendment.
  • D.C. district court dismissed; D.C. Circuit reversed; Supreme Court affirmed the Circuit.

Petitioner (District) Theory

  1. Amendment protects only militia-related gun use; ban is permissible.
  2. Even if an individual right exists, rigorous controls pass rational-basis / public-safety review.

Respondent (Heller) Theory

  1. Text (â€œthe right of the peopleâ€) guarantees a personal right independent of militia service.
  2. Total handgun prohibition and forced disablement fail any level of heightened scrutiny.

Issues

  1. Does the Second Amendment secure an individual right unconnected to militia service?
  2. If so, do D.C.â€™s handgun ban and trigger-lock requirements survive constitutional review?

Holding & Vote

2. McDonald v. City of Chicago, 561 U.S. 742 (2010)

Case Oral Summary
